THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Death of the Hon. Reginald Thomas Pollard

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 681

Tuesday, 25 August 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS Mr ANTHONY (Richmond~Acting Prime Minister)-Mr Speaker, I inform the House that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) is ill with a viral infection. During his absence I will act as Prime Minister.
DEATH OF THE HONOURABLE REGINALD THOMAS POLLARD
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Acting Prime Minister)-Mr Speaker, it is with regret that I inform the House of the death yesterday of a former member of the House, Reginald Thomas Pollard. Reg Pollard was well known to a number of members of the Parliament and I am sure that I speak for all in expressing sadness at his passing. Mr Pollard was member for Ballaarat from 1937 to 1949 and member for Lalor from 1949 to 1966. He was Minister for Commerce and Agriculture from 1946 to 1949. I am sure that there are still many people in the rural sector today who remember the work that he did on their behalf in those early post-war years. He was also involved in the general work of post-war reconstruction and acted in that portfolio for fairly long periods on two occasions.
Before entering Federal Parliament, Reg Pollard had been a member of the Victorian Parliament from 1924 to 1932 and held ministerial rank. As a glance at the parliamentary records will show, Mr Pollard served this Parliament in many ways and was a member of a range of committees. Of course, when we go back to 1915, we find that Reg Pollard enlisted in the Australian Imperial Force and went overseas with the 6th Battalion in October of that year. He was wounded in France in 1918. It can be truly said that Reg Pollard served his country with great distinction in peace and in war.
I recall Reg Pollard as a very dignified and kindly man, but on the other hand he really had a capacity to hand it out when he wanted to. He was one of those people who knew how to fight when they had to fight. I would put him in the same class as Eddie Ward, Les Haylen and even the former member for Hindmarsh, Clyde Cameron. Reg Pollard was a great stalwart of the Australi"an Labor Party and I am sure that every member of that party will remember him for many years with great affection and with great respect. He was a man who could claim to have had

a most distinguished record. In expressing my sympathy to Mr Pollard's son and his family, I move:

That this House expresses its deep regret at the death on 24 August 1981 of Mr Reginald Thomas Pollard, a member of this House for the divisions of Ballaarat, from 1937 to 1949, and Lalor, from 1949 to 1966, and Minister for Commerce and Agriculture from 1946 to 1949; places on record its appreciation of his long and meritorious public service; and tenders its profound sympathy to his family in their bereavement.

Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the

Opposition)-We heard with sadness the news of

the death of Reg Pollard. Reg Pollard was a

stalwart of the Australian Labor Party right

through the most difficult and dangerous years

that have ever faced this country. He saw the

Great Depression and what it did to the millions

of Australian workers whose interests were so

close to his heart. He. took part in the great con-

scription debates of the First and Second World

Wars and, although he served with distinction as a

volunteer for three years in the trenches in

France, where he was wounded, he remained

throughout his life a staunch opponent of forcing

young men to fight against their will. He was a

member of this House from 1937, first as the

member for Ballaarat and then as the first

member for Lalor. He served in all some 29 years

in this Parliament to 1966. He was, it is fair and

accurate to say, a veteran warrior of the worst war

in recent history-World War I. It seems to be a

rather cruel paradox that he should have paid the

price of opposing the nastiest and the most im-

moral conflict in our times-Vietnam-by being

defeated in 1966.

.

He was Minister for Commerce and Agriculture in the Chifley Government and was part of the innovative and creative team which reconstructed this country after the war, providing a
solid basis for the future of this nation. I should mention that he was again part of a highly creative and thoughtful group of men who from 1956 to 1959, as members of the Joint Committee on Constitutional Review, produced an analysis of our Constitution and prescriptions for its future
which still stand well today. Reg Pollard was a pugnacious, irrepressible, tough debating member of this Parliament; but he was also thoughtful, he was constructive and he was persistently fair. He was highly regarded for his good humour, his wit and his vitality. He was a Labor man whom Labor men were proud to call their friend. I am proud to have been able to do so. We join in extending our deepest sympathies to his surviving family. We are all deeply saddened by his passing.

Mr KILLEN (Moreton-Minister for Defence)-I was a comparatively young man

Death a/the Han. Reginald Thomas Pollard

when I came into this House, and I well recall Sir Arthur Fadden saying to me: 'You will get a lot of advice in this place. Most of it is dubious. The only advice I give you is that all of the good bowlers are not on the one team'. To have known Reg Pollard was a splendid vindication of that assessment of this Parliament. Reg Pollard was a very distinguished member of the Australian Labor Party. He was a very distinguished parliamentarian commanding immense service in this Parliament for the cause of his party and the wider cause of this country. He was a very distinguished Australian.
If I may offer a personal view, what I enjoyed immensely about him was the vigour with which he expressed his views. He expressed his views with great gusto, robustly, but in ultimate terms with enormous good humour. Nary a day went by without Reg saying a word or two to me about some issue, no matter how irrelevant or how irreverent it may have been to the political issues of the day. He had an extraordinary capacity to make his point. I can vividly recall an adjournment debate, when adjournment debates, if I may say so, with respect, counted for something. At the time he was having an argument with a Minister. Jam sorry, but I forget the Minister's name. Ministers are thoroughly transient creatures. He was the Minister in charge of war service homes.

He dumped it on the floor of the House and said: 'If this makes no impact on the Minister then the Minister is thicker than the concrete'. Three nights a week he played billiard bowls in the now private dining room with another great adversary of mine who knew how to hand out an insult, the late E. J. Ward. They were quieter, more reflective days when there was a little more leisure around this place. He enjoyed that immensely. He enjoyed this place immensely; he enjoyed the opportunity of exchanging views. As I say, he did it \Vith immense good humour, immense personal commitment and great. dignity.. Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor)-RegPollard was the-first member for Laloi' and the only one so farto die: His origins were very humble. He was born in October 1894 and educated at the Woodend State school. He joined the Australian Imperial Force in 1915 as a member of the 6th Battalion and rose through the ranks to be an officer at a time when that was very difficult to achieve. He was wounded in France and suffered
from the effects of that wound until his death. he returned to Australia in 1918 to become a soldier settler at Woodend. He was a member of the Victorian -Parliament between 1924 and 1932 as a member of the Legislative Assembly for Bulla and later Bulla-Dalhousie. He served in the Hogan Go\'ernment as Minister without Portfolio, in fact Minister for the unemployed, between 1929 and
1932.
He wrote to me once saying that he had not
taken a very active role in the AustraHan Labor Party until a by-election was held in his district. He said: 'I was ploughing one day and some blokes I knew came up and said that the local member had died and that I ought to give it a go'. That was very much Reg Pollard's style. He was knocked out of the Victorian Parliament in 1932, ran for the Federal Seat of Gippsland in 1934 and finally entered the Hous'e of Representatives in 1937 as
MHR for Ballaarat. Between 1946 and 1949 he served in the Chiftey Government as Minister for Commerce and Agriculture. In 1949, when the new seat of Lalor was carved out of the old seat of Corio, Reg Pollard was elected; he was re-elected six times;

In 1951 Reg Pollard was approached to contest

the deputy leadership of the Labor Party after the

death of Mr Chiftey and the election of Dr Evatt

to succeed him, but he decljned, although he had

strong support and might well have won. In 1960

he. contested t~e leadership against Arthur

Calwell and lost narrowly~ That defeat was one of

the great 'might-have-beens' of Australian poli-

tics. If he had won the leadership the outcome of

the 1961 election might have been different.

I

",

In 1966 at the age of 72 years he was defeated

,narrowly .on preferences ,by a freakish combi-

nation of circumstances. In that-year seven people

standing as independents contested Lalor. Most of

them were Royal Australian Air Force personnel

who wanted to get out Reg Pollard was at the

bottom of the ticket. The Liberal candidate who

won, Merv .Lee, .\Vas immediately in front of him

on the ticket and all the preferences flowed down

to him. A journalist friend of mine, John Keiley,

Death of the Hon. Reginald Thomas Pollard

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 683

tells a story about that election. Nobody thought of Lalor as a seat that Labor could lose at the 1966 election. No attention was paid to it by the media, even on election night. On election night Reg Pollard was well ahead on primaries. On the Monday morning John Keiley from the Sun rang the late J. V. McConnell, the director of the Liberal Party in Victoria and said to him: 'Have you got any comment to make on Lalor?' McConnell said: 'Lalor? What on earth is there to comment on in Lalor?' John Keiley said: 'It looks very much as if your man is going to get up in Lalor.' 'Really', said McConnell, 'and who was our man in Lalor?'
Reg Pollard was courageous and aggressive. He was avery powerful speaker, as all who heard him will testify. I heard him last at the age of 83 or so at a function at the old Customs House in Melbourne when he amazed everyone with the fervour of his speech. He was suspended from the House of Representatives several times and threatened with suspension on innumerable occasions. He was involved in a few rowdy scenes and occasional fistfights over the years but he made no enemies. He was admired as a great patriot, a great Australian and a great parliamentarian.
In 1979 a number of us, including Gough Whitlam, Frank Wilkes and Clyde Holding, made strong representations to the Council for the Order of Australia that Reg Pollard should be given the Order of Australia in recognition of his 37 years as a member of parliament and six years as a Minister. I do not think we received even an acknowledgement. There are a whole host of mediocrities and eminent forgettables who have received the Order of Australia. Reg Pollard passed froin the scene without any official recognition at all. He may not have cared. He was not the sort of man to be worried about decorations and rank; but we ought to be concerned when real merit such as Reg Pollard had goes unrecognised and unrewarded.
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry)-Firstly, as Minister for Primary Industry, I pay my tribute to the late Reg Pollard. As has been mentioned, he was Minister for Commerce and Agriculture from 1946 to 1949. There are many leaders throughout the farming community today who still recall the robustness and vigour of Reg Pollard. He had the daunting task in the post-war period of getting agriculture back on the road. He was the leader in the creation of a new wheat stabilisation plan, the fundamentals of which, without question, stand with us today. Certainly some changes have been made to it but he was the original architect of that plan. I pay tribute to him for that.

Also the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade commenced in 1946-47. He had to get Australia's viewpoint placed firmly before that and carry the worries and woes of Australia. In those days we were more isolated in a technical sense than we are today with faster moving aircraft. He also had to restart the sale of Australia's agricultural commodities in their bulk form by seeking entry to markets around the world. In all of these things he tackled problems with the same sort of vigour that I knew him personally to have .when I first came into this Parliament.
My second reason for wishing to speak is that I had an association with the late Reg Pollard through my father who was no shrinking violet in debate himself on agricultural matters at conferences when the late Reg Pollard was in attendance. When I came into this House I did so with some knowledge of the late Reg Pollard. I enjoyed very much the very vigour, already spoken about, that he put into his speeches when he stood in this place and on occasions his attacks on me as a young back bencher. The other thing that impressed me was his kindliness to a young member. After having vigorously attacked me and my party-particularly me-on one occasion he came out and said: 'Well, your father would have been proud of you there tonight, son, for the way you did it.' I forget what the argument was about at the time but, having vigorously attacked me in the House, he had the kindness to say something nice to me afterwards. I really think he was genuine in his approach to me. He had not only a robustness and vigour in this Parliament but also a kindliness many may not be aware of. I support the motion moved by the Acting Prime Minister (Mr Anthony).
Dr JENKINS (Scullin)-I support the motion before the House in regard to myoId friend the late Reg Pollard. I suppose the changing nature of electorates is reflected in the fact that the electorate of Lalor that Reg Pollard represented included most of the seat that my colleague the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) represents, most of the seat my colleague the honourable member for Burke (Dr Theophanous) represents and a very substantial part of my present seat. It is not because of that situation that I wish to speak about Reg. I had a long personal association with him, long before I entered this Parliament. I happened to J:?e one of his constituents. I happened, surprisingly enough, to be a member of his campaign committee in some of the elections he contested. My friend the honourable member for Lalor referred to the 1966 election. I can recall my wife and I being present for many days at the recount of the preferences

684 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Death a/the Han. Reginald Thomas Pollard

and the sinking feeling we had as it became obvious that our old friend, after many long years of service, was to suffer defeat.
Reg Pollard represented a parliamentarian different from the ones we are used to today. He was a parliamentarian who was interested in parliamentary debate and who would scorn the speech that was written and read out to the House. He preferred to be on his feet with his facts marshalled to do battle with his opposition on the other side. I think the days of that class of parliamentarian are passing. Reg Pollard did his job very effectively and he carried that effectiveness out into the general electorate. I attended a number of country meetings and conferences with Reg. There was one thing about accompanying him to those conferences-one always had time to reflect on the countryside around one because whether one was seated in the front of Reg's utility with him driving or being driven in another car, one never went faster than 35 miles per hour, as Reg believed that that was the maximum speed at which a vehicle should be driven.
Reg in his later life developed a much calmer approach to change in our parliamentary world. On the way to one of these meetings we were having a very heated discussion on a certain matter in the back of the car. He stopped me by saying: 'Well, doc, when I was your age'-I am now talking of some years ago-'I thought I could change the world overnight too, but I have learnt after all my years in Parliament that it is a much slower process than that'. I am finding that he was right. As well as being a good performer in Parliament he was well loved in that very vast electorate, by Victorian standards, that he represented. He was known to all groups, was well respected and was an outstanding representative of the Australian Labor Party.
Sir WILLIAM McMAHON (Lowe)-I support the motion. I do so because I think I knew the late Reggie Pollard almost as well as anyone else in the House. You will remember, Mr Speaker, and I think some other members of the House will remember, that the Department of Commerce and Agriculture was changed to become the Department of Trade and the Department of Primary Industry. I was the first Minister for Primary Industry. Naturally enough, many of the things with which I had to deal were also of very great interest to the late Reggie Pollard. It will be remembered that he introduced the first wheat stabilisation agreement in Australia. When I became the Minister I spoke to him frequently about the agreement because I felt that many changes had to be made. He agreed-with them. I remember very well that on the night when the discussion

ended and the agreement was signed by me on behalf of the Commonwealth and by representatives of the State governments, I rang him and told him of the price. He said: 'That is about 3d more than you told me about only four days ago'. I replied: 'Yes, that is so, but my permanent head was sick and I had nobody else with me. I felt that it was better to agree and to obtain the States' full approval than not.' He was ecstatic.
There was a similar occurrence in the case of the butter industry. I am sorry that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) is not able to listen to this speech because in those days I was very generous to the butter industry. We had agreed to a fairly good increase in price, and Reg phoned me to ask me about it. I said: 'Well, you know my attitude to these things. I gave them everything they wanted, so they had every right to be satisfied'. It was much the same \'{hen we gave the first increase in a base price for the butter industry. The late honourable member was delighted when such a thing happened. It showed his real feeling for the agricultural and primary industries of this country. I regarded him as a friend, a very charming person, as nice a bloke as you could find in the House. Therefore, I am glad to be able to join my colleagues on both sides of the chamber in what they have said. Also, I wish to convey my very deep respects to his relatives and to those who survive him.
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke)-I am very sadd~ned at the death of Reg Pollard who, as the honourable member for Scullin (Dr Jenkins) has mentioned, represented what is now effectively the seat of Burke as well as the seat of Lalor. He lived in my electorate, in Woodend, after his retirement. I had occasion to meet him at the end of last year when the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) came to present him with a medal to mark his many years of active service to the Australian Labor Party. It was a very emotional occasion for him to be surrounded by friends and to be presented with that medal. Although he was of a very advanced age, he charmed everyone with the stories he told about the old parliamentary days; in fact, he reminded us of just how good things were within this Parliament in those days. He offered, and gave me, his support in the last election campaign. I can only say that I regret that because of the hurly-burly of events I was not able to spend more time with him afterwards. I wish to express my deepest sympathies to his family, and especially his children.
Mr UREN (Reid)-Mr Speaker, I served with Reg Pollard in .this House from 1958 to 1966. I thought he was 70 when he was defeated but the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones)

Death of the Hon. Reginald Thomas Pollard

25 August. 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 685

said that he was 72. I will not quibble with that. All I can say is that he was the youngest 72 year old person I have known in my life. His politics were young. He was aggressive in debate. He was never a personality man; he was a policy man. I never heard him attack a man personally, but certainly he would attack policies. He was also a dedicated socialist, a fact which no one has mentioned today. He expressed his views concerning socialism and one interesting aspect is that, distinct from many who in their younger days are socialists and as they become older tend to move away from socialism, Reg Pollard was always very firm in his socialist commitment.
The election of the party leadership in 1960 was ironic. Reg Pollard was responsible for the defeat of Ward as deputy leader. In turn, Ward was responsible for the election of Calwell as It<ader. The honourable member for Lalor said that Reg Pollard was defeated narrowly. He was defeated by 42 votes to 30. That may seem to have been a wide margin but, in fact, the Ward group, which comprised six or seven members, voted for Arthur Calwell as leader. In turn, in the Deputy Leadership ballot, preferences when distributed moved the majority of votes away from Haylen to Whitlam instead of to Ward. Historically, that situation changed many things.
Few people were aware that Reg Pollard had insomnia. He rarely slept more than two or three hours a night. The rest of his time he read very widely. Generally his readings were devoted to humanity and to people. He used his great wisdom to transfer that commitment to the younger members of our party. That is why I said that I have never known a man at his age to be so young in his penetration and his activities even after he was defeated and left the Parliament. Meeting him was an inspiration for those who fight for what Chifley called the light on the hill. I have those feelings for PoUard. He gave me inspiration for the future.
He lived a wonderful and happy life with his family at Woodend. The Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) talked about the episode of fighting with what was the War Service Homes Division for a fellow whom he thought had suffered an injustice. The former Senator Sir William Spooner was the Minister involved. Reg continued that fight behind the sCenes and in the end he had to wheel that part of the foundation of his constituent's house into the Parliament. That is the type of fellow he was. I join my colleagues in addirig my respect and condolences to his family at Woodend. AU in the Parliament should have known Reg Pollard. He was an inspiration to all Australian parliamentarians..

Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes)-I want to endorse briefly the remarks that have been made. It is a sobering thought that when you, Mr Speaker, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) and I entered the Parliament in 1955, Reg had been here for 18 years. Each of us accepted him as a master craftsman. He was the most honest, uninhibited and forthright parliamentarian I have seen perform. Many people have some of those qualities, but Reg Pollard was rare in that he had them all together. I suppose it was because of that that he was able, with great frequency, to top the ballot in the parliamentary caucus room. He never aspired for very much, but whatever he sought he gained with almost the total endorsement of his colleagues. It says something for him that although he went off to fight in France and served as a serjeant, subsequently a lieutenant, was wounded and carried several bits of shrapnel in his lung, there was no greater antagonist towards conscription than Reg Pollard. In fact, it was his hard stand on that issue which resulted in his removal from the Parliament at the 1966 election which was, of course, what was known as the Vietnam election.
I often remember Reg telling stories about the immediate post-war days when he made his living from chopping wood and carting it in a cart with a horse called Dobbin to the bakery. He used to talk with great affection about a very fine old parliamentarian named Don .McLeod. If my memory serves me correctly he preceded the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) as the honourable member for Wannon.
One thing that caused Reg Pollard to be very notorious on-the special occasions around this place was that he was a great recipient of all the free cigars that came from the Royal occasions and the banquets. Honourable members from both sides of the House who did not smoke used to say, almost with one voice and in unison: 'I do not smoke, but I will take that for Reg Pollard.' He puffed away on those long cigars for a long time. I know that the late Don Whitington said of Reg Pollard that no one was more suited to succeed Ben Chifley to the leadership of the Australian Labor Party. I always believed that he could have been a leader. I am pleased to recall that, in fact, I supported him towards that end. I mourn his passing and regard the service he has given as tieing extremely valuable to the 'nation and an inspiration to many members of the Parliament.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio)-'--Mr Speaker, I would like briefly to join those honourable members who have spoken in relation to the passing of Reg Pollard. Reg was a down to earth person, a person

686 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Death o/the Hon. Reginald Thomas Pollard

who served in this Parliament sufficiently long ago that there is now no Labor member of this Parliament who served with him. You, Mr Speaker, a few Government members, including the present Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock) and Senator Chipp from Victoria did serve with him. I believe Reg Pollard's great contribution to this Parliament and this nation was his fight for organised marketing and proper stabilised conditions for primary industry. He was a victim of what was a bad resettlement scheme for soldier settlers after the First World War. He consistently fought for farmers to be given the basic right to which he considered they were entitled-a living.
I well remember the story of a referendum on introducing a floor price for wool which he, along with many others, was instrumental in having put to the people in the 1920s and the referendum was lost. Only 74.5 per cent of the votes were gained, and that was not enough to win. But Reg Pollard never changed. For as long as I can remember-I remember him in campaigns when I first stood as a candidate-he was pushing for floor prices, stabilised prices, and a basic income for farmers. He lived through the Depression, and he lived until the end of his life as a man whose basic concern was to give all Australians a fair go.
Mr MILDREN (Ballarat)-As the current member for Ballarat, it is my privilege to join in this moment of thought and reflection upon the late Reg Pollard. Reg Pollard represented the seat of Ballaarat from 1937 to 1949 and he successfully contested four elections during those very troubled years in this nation's history. He was a humble man, a man of great loyalties and a man who was totally committed to the cause of the less advantaged people in our society. Reg Pollard was a much loved man in his electorate and throughout the community. He was an assiduous worker. He never lost touch with his constituents even though he achieved the honour of membership of the great Chifley Ministry. As has been mentioned, he was Minister for Commerce and Agriculture from 1946 until 1949, and Acting Minister for Post-War Reconstruction from June to August in 1947 and from November 1947 to February 1948 during the absence overseas of another great Australian of happy memory, the Honourable J. J. Dedman.
He was a man much loved and much respected by the farming community. As indicated, he was responsible for introducing the first wheat stabilisation scheme. Reg Pollard was, in essence, a man of the people. Be never lost sight of the purpose for which he was elected. He was never one to

shun those whose lives reflected their disadvantaged state in our society. He was not a man to equivocate and perhaps that should be a lesson for many politicians. He made a comment which reflects the basic premise of his philosophy. Whilst mean spirits might quibble, the comment made at a picnic attended by stalwarts at ~urrum beet gave him direction in his politicaland personallife.Jt epitomised his love for the underdog in our society. He said:
In a capitalist society the worker is always right.
The nation and the Australian Labor Party can reflect with gratitude upon the contribution of this great Australian. I should like to pass on my deepest sympathy, and that of my party and the Ballarat electorate, to his family and relatives.
Mr SPEAKER-I remember Reg Pollard with affection, .respect and admiration. I want to add just one thing to what has been said, with all of which I agree: Reg Pollard was one of the few members of this House who were able to fill the House when they spoke.
Question so resolved in the affirmative, honourable members standing in their places.

Tertiary EdiIcation
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. The proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. The proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. In regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. The proposed restriction of adult secondary assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. The proposal not to fund 30 colleges of advanced education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid-I 980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:

688 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Petitions

(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students;
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students;
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced; (iv) that there be no restriction of adult secondary
assistance; (v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are
rationalised be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child and Mr Dawkins.

Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We, the undersigned citizens of the Commonwealth, do humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government:
I. Note that legislation establishing plant breeders' rights in other countries has had serious adverse effects, namely:
(a) Virtual monopoly control of seed production has passed into the hands of a few large international corporations seeking to profit from the exclusive rights overplant genetic materials created by such legislation.
(b) The varieties of seeds available have been restricted mainly to hybrids which will not reproduce tr~ly and will not grow without the aid of artificial fertilisers and pesticides, thus maximising corporate profits without regard for the interests of growers and consumers.
(c) The genetic diversity of crops has been eroded, rendering them vulnerable to disease and other environmimtal threats.
2. Recognise that maintenance of the genetic. diversity of plant varieties is crucial to the continued well-being of the

Petitions

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 689

Australian nation, and take all necessary steps to preserve and promote such genetic diversity as a public resource and to prevent exclusive control over plant genetic materials from faIling into private hands.
3. Defend the vital interests of Australian farmers and gar~ deners, independent Australian seed companies and their employees, and consumers of Australian farm and garden produce, by rejecting any proposal to legislate for the establishment of plant breeders' rights in Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Ca.rlton.

Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth:
That your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(I) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present) in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research 'to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.

Aboriginal Land Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the projected repeal by the Queensland State Government of the Aborigines Act 1971-1979 and the Torres Strait Islander Act 1971-1979 will almost certainly lead to the degazettal of all Aboriginal reserves in Queensland, as has already been the case with Aurukun and Mornington Island.
That if, as is likely, the Aboriginal communities from these reserves are placed under similar shire council legislation on fifty year leases, they will be exposed to serious encroachment on their land by mining and tourist interests.
That it is also quite clear, on past evidence, that these interests will have the full backing of the Queensland Government and the rights of Aboriginal people to land rights and self-management will not be granted by that Government.
That the Commonwealth Government has the power to legislate and acquire land for Aboriginal people under its constitutional powers since the amendments to the Constitution in 1967, but it is your Petitioners' sincere belief that it failed to act in 1978 arid thus failed in its duty before the people of Aurukun and Mornington Island.
That the Aboriginal people of Queensland have stated that they want land rights and self-management.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government make use of its constitutional and legislative powers to grant full land rights and self-management to the Aboriginal people of Queensland.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dawkins.

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information rliscussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in June 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that "between 12 and 16

690 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Petitions

years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent."
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
(2) That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dobie.

SALES TAX
Mr GROOM-I direct my question to the Treasurer. Why have officers of the Australian Taxation Office asked wholesalers to collect the new sales tax even though the proposed legislation has not yet become law? Who has authorised wholesalers to collect this tax? What happens if they do not? Does the Treasurer know that a large quantity of goods held up in stores and on wharves for some weeks as a direct result of the recent transport strikes will now attract the increased sales tax? Is any relief available for the purchasers of those goods, especially for those who paid for the goods before the Budget but have not yet taken delivery from the wholesalers?
Mr HOWARD-The sales tax increases were the subject of legislation introduced into this Parliament on Tuesday night last. That legislation will increase the existing rates by 2.5 per cent and change certain other rates under the general heading of a series of anomalies which were set out in the Budget Papers. The Australian Taxation Office is collecting and behaving in precisely the same way as has occurred on all previous occasions when sales tax changes have been made. I have been informed by the Commissioner of Taxation that on every previous occasion when a classification has been altered which results in a higher rate of tax and when there has been an increase in the rate of sales tax, the practice has been for enabling legislation to be introduced when the change has been announced and for that change to have effect from the date of the introduction of that legislation. In every respect the legislation which was introduced, on Tuesday last and the behaviour of the Taxation Office under that legislation follow the previous practice.
I remind the honourable gentleman that the changes I announced in the Budget which will apply, for the first time, a rate of 2.5 per cent to a category of goods presently exempted will not be imposed, of course, until 1 January 1982. As a consequence, no steps will be taken by the Taxation Office to collect the sales tax in respect of those items until that date. I was not previously aware of the circumstances described by the honourable gentleman regarding a consignment of

merchandise which was affected by a strike. Regrettably, this is another illustration of the cost to this community and to individual merchants and traders of strike action. I indicate to the, honourable gentleman that it is not possible, in the circumstances that he has described, to provide an exemption in that case from the tax in question.
HEALTH INSURANCE
Mr FREE-My question is directed to the Minister for Health. Is it correct that the Government has refused to approve Medibank Private rates in a number of States despite the fact that in at least two States these rates have been announced already and pamphlets detailing them distributed? Did the Minister reject these Medibank rates because they were actuarially irresponsible or because they threatened the private funds by undercutting them?
Mr MacKELLAR-All health funds have to submit to the registration committee their suggested rates for contributors. The registration committee comprises two officers of my Department plus the Government Actuary. Obviously the various funds have access to their actuarial advice and the Government has access to the advice of the Government Actuary. The registration committee analyses the proposals put forward by the individual funds and makes a decision based on the experience and the background of the individual fund putting forward the propositions. Unless there is a dispute between the funds and the registration committee which cannot be resolved between those two parties, as Minister for Health I do not see any of the suggested rates put forward.
So far, I have not had to adjudicate or make any decision in relation to any of the rates put forward by any of the funds. I do not anticipate having to do so as I have every confidence in the good sense of both the funds andLhe registration committee. I make the point though that the Government will not allow a situation to develop whereby contributors joining a particular fund may have their health insurance put in jeopardy because of unsound actuarial rates put forward by any individual fund.
PRIME MINISTER'S HEALTH
Mr CARLTON-will the Acting Prime Minister provide further details to the House as to the state of the Prime Minister's health?
Mr ANTHONY-When the health of a person in a high public position is in question it causes an awful lot of comment, much of which is not sympathetic. But it certainly causes a lot of speculation and mischief when a person has a record of

QUfistions Without Notice

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 697

getting ill. I suppose a person in a high position is never expected to get ill. Occasionally, some of us do get the flu and we do get sick. The Prime Minister has previously had a bout of pneumonia which would be a matter of concern to anybody. On this occasion the Prime Minister has a viral infection. His doctors have advised him that he ought to have a spell, that he ought to be fully recovered before he goes back to work because of a very heavy program ahead of him, particularly with the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting. Therefore he has decided to take three weeks off work. I think that is fair and reasonable and I am sure that anybody in normal circumstances would have at least that long a break.
HEALTH INSURANCE
Dr BLEWETT-Given the present instability of health insurance arrangements in this country, will the Minister for Health assure this House and the Australian people that after this week he will approve no further increases in contribution rates for health funds operative before 1 September 1982?
Mr MacKELLAR-It is most unfortunate that honourable members opposite should seek to cause confusion and to create doubt in the minds of the people of Australia in relation to the operation of the health insurance funds. The facts of the matter in relation to rates I have just spelt out to the honourable gentleman's associate. The amount of money which any fund has to cover, of course, depends on factors outside the direct control of the individual fund. For instance, the hospital charges which are introduced by the various State governments have a very significant impact upon the amount of pay-outs that any fund has to make. Obviously, I cannot give any assurance in relation to factors which are outside my control.
I point out that the charges which are introduced by State governments are for them to make decisions about. I also point out that a fees tribunal is due to sit in relation to doctors' charges or the scheduled fees, and obviously that will have an impact upon the amounts which health insurance funds have to payout. I hope that individual funds have taken these factors into consideration in relation to the rates that they have sought approval for under the current circumstances. I do not expect that the rates will change in the near future. It would be wrong of me to give an assurance that there could not be any change, but I do not expect-I would be very surprised-to see any changes in the rates as approved from 1 September.

SALES TAX
Sir WILLIAM McMAHON-My question is directed to the Treasurer. It concerns the actual market cost to the consumer of the increase in the wholesale sales tax of 2+ per cent. Does he agree that the 2+ per cent tax at the wholesale level will be added to the former retail price which will then be the basis on which the retailer's margin will be added, with a consequent effect on the final price? Is he also aware that, in the real life of normal traders and in the inflationary conditions that exist today, the usual market response is to increase the mark-up and, therefore, the final price to the consumer and that this applies particularly to the cornerstore? Against this background, does he think it probable, as I do, that the increased cost to the consumer on average will be close to $3.50 a week, or more, rather than the official estimate of $1.50 to $2 a week? Was today's Australian Financial Review accurate in stating that the Treasurer said yesterday that the 0.8 per cent impact on the consumer price index had been calculated by the Australian Bureau of Statistics? Will he confirm that if this was so, it was a novelty?
Mr HOWARD-The answer to the first part of the right honourable gentleman's question is that, of course, the final price to the consumer will be affected by the retailer's mark-up. The extent to which that mark-up occurs will be determined critically by conditions of competition which exist in different parts of the retail sector in respect of different items which are affected by the sales tax changes. Obviously, if conditions of greater competition exist, the pressure constraining retail mark-ups will be greater. Unfortunately, there are some areas where competition does not exist to the optimum degree and, as a result, the retail mark-ups will be greater.
There is already some evidence in respect of certain products that competition will act to restrain the degree of mark-up. In one or two cases, I have noticed announcements to the effect that the 2+ per cent increase will be absorbed, for the time being, by certain traders. That underlines a point I made in reply to a question last week-it is very difficult t'o estimate with precision the impact of these changes on an average family. The figure that I gave of $1.50 to $2 a week is one that I still view as practical and appropriate. I repeat what I said last week: In order for the figure of $3.50 a week to be valid a consumer would need to spend more than half of average household disposable income on items attracting sales tax. In order for an increased amount of $3.50 to be attributed to the sales tax rises total expenditure on affected items would have to be about $140 a

making CPI estimates. I am not sure that that is a novelty but I will examine the previous practice to see whether my understanding of the practice that was adopted this time is in fact wrong.

JET NETWORK AIR FARES: REPORT
Mr SHACK-I refer the Minister for Transport to the report of Price Waterhouse Associates Pty Ltd on jet network air fares which he tabled in this place last Thursday. The Minister will recall that in the covering letter to the report, the Director of Price Waterhouse wrote:
Our recommended formula for immediate use is provided in a confidential report under separate cover.
Will the Minister advise the House what is the Price Waterhouse recommended air fare formula? Will he make available to the House the confidential report he has received from Price Waterhouse?
Mr HUNT-The Price Waterhouse report recommended the abolition of the two-tier system, which is to the advantage of long-haul travellers. Indeed if the Government had adopted the approach of the Price Waterhouse report, fares between the eastern States and Western Australia would have risen by 12.6 per cent rather than the 8 per cent which was the average increas~ that was justified by the airlines in the last submission. I am not able to table the confidential report because of the confidentiality of the material that was provided. I have checked with Price Waterhouse. It has asked that the confidentiality of that information be maintained. However, I will table a letter that related to the confidential report which was written to the Secretary to the Department of Transport. The recommended flag-fall and distance rate are set out. For the benefit of the House I will read from a paragraph of the letter, which states:
Within the constraints outlined above we would recommend that the values set out for the first half of the year, or until review by IAFC, should be somewhere near the simple average of the two prices. That is $42.14 flag-fall and 7.293 cents per kilometre. This does not take into account the sec: ond tier distance rate. There should be an adjustment to our

price in the future to enable the recommended price to move toward the average price level expected for budgeted cost recovery over the year ended 30 June 1982, or to take account of
unbudgeted cost rises.

One of the reasons why the Government did not pursue the Price Waterhouse approach on this occasion was that there was a difference in the way in which Price Waterhouse allocated some of the cost inputs to the flag-fall as against the distance rate. This varied from the approach that the Holcroft inquiry adopted. Of course the question of cross-subsidisation of F27 services has not been dealt with at all. So, in fairness to all parties, it was resolved that. there should be an average
adjustment on this occasion. The Price Waterhouse report and the Holcroft report, of course, will be the subject of the inquiry as soon as the independent air fares committee is set in operation. There is no doubt that there will have to be
a public inquiry. The question of the flag-fall and the distance rate will naturally be the subject of a very close and intensive investigation by that public inquiry as soon as it comes into operation.

Mr Hurford-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. Could we ask the Minister to incorporate the whole letter in Hansard?

Mr HUNT-If you wish, yes.

Leave granted.

The letter read as follows-

25 August 1981

Mr C.C. Halton Secretary Department of Transport P.O. Box 367 CANBERRA CITY ACT 2601

PRICE WATERHOUSE ASSOCIATES PTY
Management consultants

Dear Sir, DOMESTIC AIR FARES FORMULA RECOMMENDATION

In accordance with your request we set out below information ancillary to our recent report on an air fares formula.

During our study we reviewed the historical cost (year

ended 3d June 1981) and budgeted cost (year ended 30 June

1982) of both airlines. TAA gave us permission to release all

the information including budgets for your confidential use.

However, Ansett requi~ed us to withhold any information re-

lating to their budgets.

.

The formula values recommended below are sufficient to allow the higher cost airline, TAA, a breakeven on budgeted costs and to allow Ansel!, the lower cost airline, a reasonable level of profit acceptable to both themselves and us.

The airline formula recommended by us aims at recovering airline operating costs in the terms of the two airline agree_ ment and at the same time maintaining airline viability.

The. values listed below are within the range from the cur.
rent price of $40.00 flagfall and 6.848 cents pllr kilometre distance rate" and the higher levels of $44.27 flagfail and 7.739

700 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Questions Without Notice

cents p~r kilometre which would be the average price necess-
ary to provide T AA with a breakeven on costs over the budget period which covers I July 1981 to 30 June 1982.
We have ignored the second tier rate for the purpose of this exercise because we agree with Holcroft that there is no accounting justification for such a complication to the formula.
It is worth noting that in the near future neither airline might pay taxes mainly because of the availability of investment allowances and in T AA 's case possible carry forward tax 10!lSes. Consequently the level of pretax profit allowed in the formula could also be looked on as fully distributable to shareholders/owners.
Within the constraints outlined above we would recommend that the values set out for the first half of the year, or until review by IAFC, should be somewhere near the simple average of the two prices. That is $42.14 flagfall and 7.293 cents per kilometre. This does not take into account the second tier distance rate. There should be an adjustment of our price in the future to enable the recommended price to move toward the average price level expected for budgeted cost recovery over the year ended 30 June 1982, or to take account of unbudgeted cost rises.
Our recommended formula rate is based on the assumption that it is the rate requested for the whole of the first half of the accounting year. An adjustment upwards may be necessary to take into account the lateness of the air fare application to the network in this period.
Yours faithfully
AJ McKinnon
Director
REFUGEE STATUS
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Has the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs received a report on the inquiry being conducted by a United States task force into the status of refugees from the South East Asian region, specifically as to whether these refugees fall within the definition created by various internationallegal instruments and recognised by the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, or whether they are fleeing for mainly economic reasons and therefore may not be eligible for entry into the United States? What is the attitude of the Australian Government in respect of this matter?
Mr MACPHEE-I have had reports from Australian posts on discussions they have had with both the congressional mission and another mission headed by Mr Marshall Green on behalf of Congress. The inquiries reveal that the picture is a very complex one, It would seem that some people are leaving primarily for economic reasons, others are fleeing a possible draft, and were too young at the end of the Vietnam war to be genuinely called refugees, and others again are defiriitely still refugees. The situation is very complex. It is somewhat academic, if people actually leave by boat, to say that they are not refugees when we know very well that if they were--

Mr Hayden-But there are very few who are true political refugees today. That is incontestable. With respect, you have said that on the record.
Mr MACPHEE-Certainly. I am not denying that. I think that very few of them would now fall into the category of political refugees. I am saying that to send them home where they might be persecuted might then make them refugees,
Mr Hayden-But they send them home to China from Hong Kong and a host of other regions. Cut it out.
Mr SPEAKER-The Leader of the Opposition will remain silent.
Mr MACPHEE-Mr Speaker, if the Leader of the Opposition wants it on his head and on his conscience to send home people who might then be persecuted, so be it. I am saying to the Parliament that there is no easy answer to this complex question. What is done in Hong Kong or elsewhere is not the business of this Government. The Government is currently discussing the matter in conjunction with other settlement countries-the United States and Canada in particular-and in conjunction with the member countries of the Association of South East Asian Nations, especially Malaysia and Thailand, and the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees. When there is something further to be said about any aspect of Government policy I shall do so, In the meantime the situation is of concern, This Government, in conjunction with the settlement countries and the countries of first asylum, is endeavouring to adopt humane deterrents which will limit the outflow.
I am bound to say that since the monsoon rains came the outflow has dropped considerably, This adds force to the suggestion that the people in question are not so much political refugees, In the days when they were predominantly political refugees they took to the seas even in the monsoon season. In March, April and May the outflow was very great. It must be a cause of international concern. It cannot be easily remedied by simply categorising people in a certain fashion.
Mr Lionel Bowen-More aid for Vietnam would help.
Mr MACPlJEE-We have opened up discussions with Viocnam on an orderly departure program. Senior officials of the Australian Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs went to Vietnam last month. We are pleased with the progress that has been made. Vietnam is now having similar discussions with the United States and Canada. At-the end of those discussions I will have more to report to the Parliament.

REFUGEE STATUS
Mr HAYDEN-My question is directed to the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs and follows his response to the question asked of him by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition. Is it a fact that people fleeing Communist China into Hong Kong when apprehended usually-I think in all cases-are returned to Communist China? Is it a fact that their position, their vulnerability and the dangers they face upon return are no greater nor less than those which the Minister suggested would confront non-political refugees fleeing Vietnam who happen to be returned? In those circumstances, can we take it as a principle of consistency that in future people who flee Communist China who are not political refugees, rather than being returned, can anticipate that the Australian Government will expand its program of refugee resettlement to allow them to settle here? Furthermore, as an ancilliary consideration, will the Minister also outline what prospects the Government has in mind for the settlement of the very large number of Afghanistan refugees who have fled into Pakistan probably with a higher claim to political refugee status and whose presence is a severe economic strain on the Pakistan Government and the Pakistan economy? In short, I ask the Minister to declare consistent and comprehensive guidelines so that there can be no confusion as to what the Government is about as distinct from political humbuggery?
Mr MACPHEE-The Governmen t has always been consistent. I will repeat its position. We work through the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees. We have had no request that I am aware of on behalf of that authority or anybody else to take any refugees who are fleeing

China. If any have been returned, they have not been returned by the Australian Government because I know of no occasion when we have been asked to take such people.
Mr Hayden-You are not answering the question directly.
Mr MACPHEE-I am answering the question directly. I listened to the honourable member's question in silence and he can listen to my answer of equal length.
Mr Hayden-Don't be a blustering humbug.
Mr SPEAKER-The Leader of the Opposition will remain silent. I call the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs.
Mr MACPHEE-If there is any humbug it is from the Leader of the Opposition.
Mr Humphreys-Answer the question.
Mr MACPHEE-I am delighted to answer the question. The position is that we have not been requested by the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees to take people from Afghanistan. The international policy there is to bring peace to Afghanistan and to repatriate the people when peace has been restored. In the meantime Pakistan-whilst it is hardly delighted to have so many refugees-is content with the international assistance given by a number of governments through the United Nations, including the Australian Government. My colleague, the Minister for Foreign Affairs, has announced to this Parliament the high level of aid which we have given under that program. We have not been requested by the United Nations High Commissioner to take refugees from Pakistan, from Somalia or from many other parts of the world from which people are displaced. We have said that we will take genuine refugees from Indo China because they were displaced and the countries to which they went asked that we take them.
As to those refugees who were in fact land people-as in the case of the people to whom the Leader of the Opposition has referred in Afghanistan and China-it is much easier for land people to be held in camps and then repatriated. In the case of boat people it is demonstrably more difficult. I would not rule out the possibility that the Australian Government and other governments concerned could negotiate with Vietnam to repatriate voluntarily some people with certain international assurances. They have not yet been sought in that sense and not yet been obtained. It is certainly a delicate problem. Our main concern must be for the well being of the individuals concerned. It is estimated by the Malaysians that over 40 per cent of those who set out from Vietnam to

702 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

- Questions Without Notice

Thailand and Malaysia have perished in the sea. I certainly do not want to take any premature action which might jeopardise the lives of others who, for whatever reason, set forth from Vietnam by boat.
AIR TRAFFIC CONTROLLERS
Mr JULL-My question is directed to the Minister for Transport and it refers to the continuing saga of the air traffic controllers in Brisbane. Is the Minister aware that the new Qantas Brisbane Tokyo service is now in jeopardy because of the shortage of air traffic controllers and that the Queensland tourist industry stands to lose 10,000 interstate and overseas visitors annually if the airport is closed for three hours per day? What is being done in the short term and indeed in the long term to overcome this most incredible situation?
Mr HUNT-Needless to say, I am most concerned about the effect of the shortage of air traffic controllers at Brisbane Airport. .
Mr Humphreys-Why don't you do something about it?
Mr HUNT-In fact we are doing something about it through a program of trying to obtain from overseas as many air traffic controller trainees as possible. We have a recruitment program here as well.
Mr Scholes-You might be able to get some from America.
Mr HUNT-We do not discount any possibility. A number of options currently are under consideration, one of which is varying the roster principles and also the shift work operations and overtime provisions of the presen( arrangements affecting air traffic controllers. Discussions are taking place with the Public Service Board regarding concessions for a limited duration in order to ease the immediate problems in Brisbane. One of the great difficulties we have with the controllers there is that many of them have in fact been working for two years without recreation leave, and in the terms of the conditions under which they have to operate they must take their leave at the end of the second year. This has contributed to a shortfall of air traffic controllers in Brisbane as from 10 September. Quite clearly, every effort is being made to transfer air traffic controllers from other centres, where possible, but, unless I can get some flexibility within the arrangements applying as a result of the Public Service Board conditions, of course I will not be able to achieve the objective of easing the difficulty in Brisbane.
I hope that there will be some positive result from those discussions with the Public Service

Board. Clearly there will be some restriction as from 10 September. It has been arranged so that there will be no restrictions whatever over the Christmas period, but the first report that came to me indicated that there would indeed be a threehour restriction between 9 o'clock and 12 o'clock from 10 September to 30 June 1982. Quite obviously, that is not satisfactory to Brisbane, to the airline system or to the travelling public, and, obviously, alternative arrangements need to be made. I repeat that I am concerned about the matter. The Government has every reason to be concerned about it. The Premier of Queensland, the Queensland Minister for Tourism and a number of other people, including all the Queensland Federal members of this parliament, have been in touch with me. Rest assured that we will do everything in our power-except moving heaven and earth-to try to overcome this very embarrassing difficulty.

COMMUNITY YOUTH SUPPORT SCHEME
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The callous disregard for the plight of unemployed young Australians reflected in the Government's decision to abandon the Community Youth Support Schem~.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their placeS.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr HAWKE (Wills) (3.28)-There may be legitimate room for argument in our community as to what are the ap-propriate economic policies to be pursued in this country. I suggest that there can be no room for the callous and deceitful abandonment of the people who are the victims of those policies, particularly the young people of this country. That, of course, is precisely what is involved in the dismantling by the Government in the Budget of the Community. Youth Support Scheme and of tbe Government's prevarications in regard to what it has done which, of course, has been added to by the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) in the Parliament this afternoon. First of all, let us get the
-

facts straight as to what is the present condition. I suggest that, presumably, an impeccable source as to the facts is contained in the last available Department of Employment and Youth Affairs' annual report for 1979-80. The Government there states:
The Community Youth Support Scheme (CYSS) was introduced in November 1976 to provide a supportive environment to help young people develop and maintain skills and job orientation while unemployed. Finance is provided to representative community committees to run projects which involve young people in a range of employment-oriented and skills development programs, recreational activities and community service work.
Its objectives are to maintain the employability and a sense of purpose and direction of young people until they find jobs. These young people are helped to continue their job search and development of job skills. CYSS has expanded rapidly since its inception and a review and rationalisation of the scheme during the 1979-80 financial year consolidated its progress and provided a sound base on which future community-based projects can be developed.
That is a clear statement of the history, the purpose and value of CYSS. The report of the Government's own Department goes on to point out that since the Scheme's inception at the end of 1976 more than 130,000 people have been assisted. The figures are listed. I will not go through them except to point out that for the year ended June 1980, 274 projects fulfilled these purposes and functions. It was not an employment placement program but, essentially, a supportive program for young people who were the victims of unemployment. To bring that picture up to date I point out the picture for the year ended 1980-81. In the period 1980-81, so far as can be ascertained, some 55,000 young people were assisted in the ways pointed out by this report, so that that would give a total, since its inception, of about 200,000 people. To sustain that, there were 276 projects right around this country employing between 800 and 850 people as project officersabout 80 per cent of which were full time-200 ancillary staff and over 1,000 people who provided part-time services as sessional instructors to help the approximately 50,000 young people who were victims of unemployment. That is what we are talking about. We are not talking about something peripheral but something deliberately created by the Government to deal with a problem at the end of 1976.
If there has been a decision to abandon the scheme, I assume one could ask oneself the question: 'Has this been done because the unemployment situation is improving?' Of course, we know that the answer to that is in the negative. What are the facts? The facts are that currently 117,000 teenagers are unemployed. That is an unemployment rate amongst teenagers of 15 per cent. In the period since the Scheme was introduced-after all

706 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Community Youth Support Scheme

this Scheme was created primarily to' deal with the problems of young people who had a longer term problem of unemployment-the average period of unemployment of teenagers has, in fact, doubled to a point where the average period of unemployment is now 25 weeks. In fact, for those in the 20 to 24 years of age group the duration has more than trebled from 10 weeks to 35 weeks. '
What has been the picture of employment creation in this period? This is the statistic which I believe is probably quite unknown to honourable members of this House and it will come as a very considerable surprise. From the end of 1975 until the latest period, July 1981, there has been an addition of only 11,000 permanent full time jobs for teenagers-that is, compared with the end of 1975 there are now only 11,000 more full time jobs for teenagers. Nearly the whole of the increase has taken place in part time employment. That is the situation. In November 1976 the, Government brought in CYSS because it saw a worsening unemployment situation amongst young people. In the period from the end of 1976 until now, when the Government decided to abolish the Scheme, in fact the position has got worse.
What is the position which the Budget predicts for the coming period? Does it perceive an improving position? Of course it does not. The opposite is true. It sees a reduction in the employment growth rate from 2.6 per cent in 1980-81 to 2 per cent in 1981-82. It predicts, in fact, an increase in unemployment. The figures for the Australian Bureau of Statistics which have just come out in the last few days show an annual employment growth which has fallen to lO9,000. In fact, that represents a very significant decline from the peak figure of 12 months ago when the annual employment growth rate was 212,000. In other words, the situation with which this community is faced is, on the figures produced by the Government, at which we are looking, a deteriorating employment position, both in the predictions as to the rate of employment growth and the level of unemployment.
What does the Government seek to do in this situation in an attempt to justify its abandonment of this Scheme which has served the community well? In a deceitful fashiort the Government has attempted to argue that, in fact, it has made a massive increase in funds available in manpower and training areas and that, in fact, the young people-until this point the 55,000 who have been serviced by CYSS-will be able to be looked after , by the additional funds made available in the training area. '

Let us look at the facts and distinguish them from the deceitfulness which has been advanced by the' Government. The facts are that from 1975-76 until last financial year there has been a 58 per cent decline in real terms in the funds made available in the manpower, training and support program area. In fact, the increase' in money terms, and in the real terms it has made available in this coming financial year, will still leave this situation that in 1981-82, as compared with 1975-76, there will have been a 44 per cent decline in real terms in the funding by this Government of its total manpower, training and support programs. What does the increase that is involved this year do? Does it in fact provide a substitution in any way for what has been done under the CYSS program. I ask honourable members to contrast the fact that some 55,000 people were serviced-that is the way in which the Government talked about it in its annual report-or looked after in 1980-81 with the numbers to be added under the training programs by the provision of the additional funds. Making the most generous assumptions, at the very most there will be an additional 20,000 places for young people available in 1981-82 as a result of the expansion of funds. I repeat: At the very most, an additional 20,000 places will be created for young people.
But, of course, the major beneficiaries of those additional funds will riot be the long term unemployed kids; they will be basically the kids who are coming straight from school or virtually straight from school. If honourable members turn to the one program that the Government talks aboutin the papers associated with the Budget, the one program which can be said to deal with the problem of long term young unemployed-that is the extended Special Youth Employment Training Program-they will find that the additional funds are expected in 1981-82 to provide for only an additional 5,750 ,places. In other words, when we analyse the Governmenfs argument that the abolition of CYSS can be justified by the provision of additional funds made available under these other programs, we find that in, the one program that can be said to be relevant less than 6,000 new places will be provided. If it is said that some people have used some of the services more than once, in fact we are comparing 6,000 in the extended SYETP with at least 30,000 to 35,000 who have been looked after in the CYSS situation. In those circumstances is it any wonder that we have been inundated with complaints not merely from the people who are working in CYSS, but also from other people in the community who have seen the benefits o( this program? We have been inundated with requests
-

Mr HAWKE-I do not think that this tragic human situation is helped by the stupid, unhelpful and inhumane objections from the honourable member for O'Connor.
