We are dealing with the real human tragedy of young people who are filled with despair and hopelessness and for whom the CYSS program was intended. To a large extent CYSS has done something to alleviate that human tragedy. That is what we are trying to do.
Mr TuckeyWhat about mum and dad?
Mr HAWKE-We do not have to depend upon these inane interjections from these people who have a record of bashing Aborigines. We do not have to listen to these interjections from that source at all. The fact is that we have had the assessment of these schemes by people who are qualified to make an assessment. The Department of Employment and Youth Affairs itself, in 1977, made an assessment and it found that the Scheme had in fact provided considerable gains for the participants in terms of personal and social development, confidence, morale and motivation. These were the findings of the Department itself. This was what these schemes had in fact done for young people faced with this despair and frustration of unemployment.
We not only have the assessment of the Department itself but also assessments made from within the Melbourne University and the Swinburne College of Advanced Education. I refer briefly to the report in the Age of Monday, 24 November on the assessment that was made by Miss Doreen Parsons, a graduate of the Swinburne College of Technology's Psychology Department. The finding there was that:

Unemployed young people involved in the Community Youth Support Scheme projects are less likely to commit crimes than those who are not involved.
In other words, a comparison was made between young people who had the opportunity of being involved in CYSS projects and programs and those kids who had not had the opportunity of being engaged in them. In that assessment it was found that these programs-not in aggregate economic terms; we are not worried about them; but in terms of what they do for the personal problems of young-kids-were doing something to maintain the morale, to maintain the feeling in these kids that there was something better to do than to resort to crime, to hard drugs and that sort of thing.
As my speaking time has just about expired, Mr Deputy Speaker, may I nail this Minister, in terms of what credibility he has when he talks in this area? I refer to an article in the Advertiser of 2 June 1981 headed 'No fear on youth schemes: Brown'. It states
Commonwealth-funded youth support schemes need not fear coming changes to funding operations, the Federal Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, Mr Brown, said yesterday.
That was on 2 June 1981. The article continued:
Mr Brown said an announcement would be made in a couple of months about changes in guidelines for the schemes, of which there were more than 250 in Australia.
. Hesaid-
That is the Minister-

The scherrte is no more than tokenism, confined to creating drop-in centres, providing 'cuddle-therapy' rather than the

jobs young people really need. The guidelines even limit training, preventing formal educational classes or instruction in jOb skills as normally provided by educational or training insti- ' tutions. The $12.26 million would be better spent on genuine job creation and training programmes.
Mr N. A. BROWN~Let me make it plain that we do not agree with the scorn and opprobrium that the Labor Party has poured on CYSS project officers and people in local and State committees. It is time that Labor Party members woke up to themselves about this matter. We will terminate the scheme on 31 October because circumstances have changed and young people are in need of skills, training and work experience. We have in the Budget increased enormously the funds which are being devoted to those worthwhile purposes.
I have said that circumstances have changed. They have done so in this way: Unemployment, including unemployment for young people, has decreased. We have never maintained that the situation is satisfactory. But there have been great gains in teenage employment in the past year. Total teenage employment grew by 14,100 or 2.1 per cent between July last year and July this year. In the same period, the level of teenage unemployment fell by 18,500 or 17 per cent. We say that that is not good enough but we also say that young people, in order to get jobs and keep them, need training in the skills, work experience and abilities that employers need and will insist on before they take on those young people as employees. Accordingly, in this Budget we have made it plain that we will not proceed with this scheme after 31 October; rather we will increase the number of places available to young people to get work experience, basic job training and training in the skills that they need to get jobs and keep them.
Not only have we increased the number of places available, but also we have increased the funds available by an enormous amount. Let me tell honourable members of the amounts with which we are dealing. Expenditure on all Department of Employment and Youth Affairs programs has been increased by 23 per cent. Of this amount, 77 per cent is for apprenticeship or other training programs directed at young people. Expenditure on youth training programs will rise by 67 per cent from last year. It will be $79.4m this year which is a remarkable achievement in terms of Government expenditure. It indicates beyond any doubt whatsoever the commitment that the Government is prepared to make to put large sums of money into training young people and giving them the work experience, skills and abilities that they need to get jobs and to keep thein.

We must face the real situation that there are young people in the Community Youth Support Scheme who have to have brought to their notice what these schemes are, how they work and what-
the prerequisites are for entry into them. Because I am concerned and the Government is concerned
about unemployment amongst young people, it is
my intention to ensure that young people have brought to their attention the wide ranging ambit of schemes which are available for them to. get work experience and to acquire skills to help them find and keep jobs. Departmental officials from my Department will be visiting all the CYSS projects. It will be explained to the project officers and to the participants in the scheme exactly what the Government's programs are, how those programs work, what the trainee allowances are and what facilities are available to them in their own areas so that they can take some positive action to acquire those skills, work experience and abilities which they need to obtain and to keep employment.

710 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Community Youth Support Scheme

That is the situation. The machinery, the funds and the programs are there. What is needed is an awareness amongst young people and those who advise them as to what our programs are and how young people can take part in them. Those visits will be made. Counselling will be given to young people and they will benefit from it. I also emphasise that young people should visit the Commonwealth Employment Service. They should use the services available there. If they do, they will find that the Special Youth Employment Training places are on the CES boards. They will find that the jobs are there. They will find that they'can get work experience and advice by using the CES.
Mr Hawke-That is a deliberate untruth.
Mr N. A. BROWN-They will find that the jobs are there. In case the honourable member did not hear, I repeat that they will find that there are specific jobs identified as Special Youth Employment Training Program jobs. They will get all the advice and all the help that they need to get themselves involved in one of the training schemes, one of the work experience programs, which are there. I must also say that we will be able to make the assets of the schemes available. to other organisations involved in youth work in the locality. The attempt by the honourable member for Wills to bring this matter on as a matter of public importance is tainted with hypocrisy because it is completely inconsistent with and clean contrary to the whole attitude of the Labor Party on this matter since the day CYSS was conceived.
I make this final point, and it is a very good opportunity to make it: This debate and this argument have been a salutary lesson, I would suggest, to the people of Australia, particularly to those interested in the issues of employment and unemployment. The salutary lesson-it is about time that more Australian people woke up to it-is that people will employ other people will employ other people if it is profitable for them to do so and if the people they are contemplating employing have the skills, abilities, experience and training that the employer wants. They will not employ people out of charity. They will not employ people because we ask them to do so. They will employ people if it is profitable and economic to do so and if the people available have the skills that they need. They will not do so otherwise. We say, therefore, that a government can do two things. Firstly, it can create the right economic environment in which it will be profitable to employ, as employers are now doing. The second thing a government can do is to equip people, particularly young people, with the skills, work experience, training and abilities that they need to acquire a job and to keep it.

Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (4.0)-The speech that I have just been unfortunate enough to hear from the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A.Brown) must rate as the most cynical utterance that I have heard in my short time in Federal Parliament. The Minister obviously has. not had close contact, with the kids in. the Community Youth Support Scheme or he would not have wasted the entire time that he had to speak in trying to pour heaps of coal onto the Australian.Labor Party. He has repeated this statement on

a number of occasions, and I believe from these reports that his utterances were so cynical as to indicate that he was staging an argument which he did not even believe in, that he did not want CYSS scrapped, and that he was rolled in Cabinet.
Mr Deputy Speaker, it is unfortunate that you are in the chair. I am casting no reflection on the Chair, but I wish to quote some of your words which appeared in your local paper, the Gympie Times, on Saturday, 22 August. The article states:
The Treasurer's brief statement signalling the end of CYSS came as a complete shock to you.
The story further says that you 'had been impressed at the level of performance attained at the local CYSS centre' and that you 'remain unconvinced that the 'decision fairly recognised the value ofCYSS in particular localities'. I commend you for those sentiments, which I share. I say to those to date mute honourable members in this chamber who, outside this chamber and in their electorates, have joined with their Labor colleagues in expressing dismay and concern at the demise of yet another social welfare project: 'Where do you stand in this chamber? Will your voices be raised in protest against this injustice? Where will you be when a vote is taken on the future of CYSS?,
There are 15 CYSS centres operating in the Brisbane metropolitan area, which covers nine Federal electorates, five of which are held by Government members. These members have had seven days to consider their position since the terse, two-sentence Budget announcement by the Treasurer that the Community Youth Support Scheme is to be discontinued on 31 October 1981. I hope that the honourable members from those electorates will take a stand in this chamber. I hope that they will not adopt the habit of saying one thing in their electorates and taking an entirely contradictory course of ac~ion in ~this House, where their votes and their voices really count. I am, therefore, calling on those Government members with moral conviction and conscience to cross the floor of this House to save CYSS when the vote is taken following the Budget debate. Our young unemployed Australhms need the support of honourable members on both sides of this chamber.
In all .the treachery that has been perpetrated on the kids of this country, the kids who have been sacrificed on the altar of the Government's economic policy, no greater injustice has been done than the abandonment of CYSS. The Government's failure to increase unemployment benefits for those under 18 years of age from an incredible $36 per week-$52 below the poverty line-was

712 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Community Youth Support Scheme

bad enough. The passive acknowledgment of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) that his Budget strategy would deliberately increase unemployment was bad enough. But the unkindest cut of all is that the - very program which would have helped in some way to relieve the anxiety and real poverty caused by increasing unemployment and decreasing unemployment benefit-the CYSS program-has been axed. The plight of those under-18s who are without parental support for whatever reason will now become unbearable without the safety valve of the local CYSS centres.
Youth homelessness in our cities is now in epidemic proportions. In my electorate of Lilley, street kids are sleeping in a hostel overnight and wandering around during the day. They are people without parental support. It is something that some Government members appear unable to understand from their privileged position in this chamber. When I raised the question on Wednesday night I had to put up with inane comments such as: 'Where are the parents?' Some honourable members are quite unaware that some kids have parents living in the country areas. Some do not have parental support. Some parents, because they are on such low incomes, cannot afford to keep their children at home or to keep them further at educational institutions. Honourable members should ask any church or community organisation which works with the young.
I make this solemn prediction. If the attentions of the Government are not directed immediately to the youths of this country and the crisis they face and to their increasingly aimless and hopeless existence, violence such as that which occurred at Brixton may be as common on the streets of Brisbane, Sydney and Melbourne as it is in London and Liverpool. How do. honourable members think the violence started there~ It was born out of unemployment and fostered by the indifference of politicians who would not stand up for what they believed in and bureaucratswho could not be truste.d. It was nurtured by social apathy and the derisive platitudes of 'dole bludger',
I am particularly concerned about the effects of OYSS cuts on the hundreds of y01,lng people who have been helped by CYSS centres such as Windsor and Deagon in my electorate. The project officers and staff at these centres, as in other centres around the country, have made an invaluable contribution to the often aimless existence of young people who through no fault of their own, find themselves unemployed. The CYSS centres have saved many of these people from falling into the trap of perpetual unemployment. Our unemployed youth have nowhere to turn. They need the confidence and backing of

their peer group. They need social workers and adults who will stand behind them. Once CYSS is scrapped how can we legitimately tell our young people that we sincerely care about their future and welfare? Our credibility as parents and politicians will be shot to pieces.
Towards the end of 1977,116 CYSS programs were under way around Australia involving 15,000 unemployed kids. If CYSS has been ineffective why has the Government continued to grant approval to those projects and an additional 180 projects? The proliferation of CYSS centres has served to heighten the expectations of the young unemployed. I point out that the other training schemes which are being vaunted do not meet the needs of the kids who will not be accepted. They cannot all be accepted, due to their large numbers. The Minister knows this. There will still be kids unemployed who will not have the support that they now have from CYSS centres which, by the Minister's words, are effective, are workable and should have extra funding in order to keep them going. The only thing that the Labor Party has said in the past is that we should have jobs rather than training for these kids.
Let us remember that we are talking about young people. They are the most vulnerable element in our society. 'Care for Kids' was 'last year's theme. How soon we forget. We had that theme in an election year. Could honourable members imagine the opposition to this Budget decision if it occurred in the midst of the 'Care for Kids' campaign? Mr CHAPMAN (Kingston) (4.12)-The emotional tirade that we have just heard from the honourable member for Lilley (Mrs Darling) has failed to provide any rational argument against the Government's decision to abolish the Community Youth Support Scheme or in support of the matter of public importance raised by the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke), namely:
The callous disregard for the plight of unemployed young Australians reflected in the Government's decision to abandon the Community Youth Support Scheme.
This matter of public importance total demonstrates, as the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Me N. A. Brown) said earlier, the hypocrisy of the Australian Labor Party and the honourable member for Wills who raised it. As the Minister indicated, the Labor Party has continually criticised CYSS and its operation, yet now that it has been abolished the Opposition suddenly jumps to its defence. I quote again from the book by the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young) I want to Work, where he clearly states:
The scheme, with a budget of $12.26 million in 1978-79, is to Fund community-based organisations to run activities For unemployed youth . . . .
The $12.26 million would be better spent on genuine job creation and training programs.
That is exactly the decision that the Government has taken in this Budget. It has transferred the funds that have been previously allocated to the CYSS projects to other training programs and furthermore it has significantly increased those training programs by 67 per cent. I will refer to that later in further detail.
The hypocrisy of the Labor Party is further demonstrated by the fact that a government of its persuasion presided over the greatest destruction of jobs in Australia since the Great Depression. Between August 1973 and August 1975 uriemployment increased from 67,200 to 215,500 while the recipients of unemployment benefits went up from 38,000 to over 160,000. The number of unemployed increased by 210 per cent and the number of unemployment benefit recipients by 324 per cent. During the same period the number employed in the private sector declined rapidly by 38,000.

So when the Fraser Government came to office in 1975 it was faced with the massive task of overcoming this disastrous situation. Through sound economic management, which included restraint on government spending and the urging of wage restraint, it has restored the climate for economic growth and thereby restored job growth. Slowly but surely success has been achieved and Labor's disastrous record has been reversed.
Employment in the last 12 months increased by 146,300 and over the last two years by 321,400. That growth in employment has been a consistent trend over that period. During the same period unemployment declined from 324,800 ~n July 1980 to 315,000 in July 1981. These favourable results have also been reflected in significant improvement in youth unemployment. Youth unemployment over the last two years has declined from 20 per cent to 15 per cent. This year 85 per cent oflast year's schoolleavers had obtained employment by the middle of the year, compared with 80 per cent in 1980. Once again that demonstrates a significant improvement in the employment situation for young people. The best thing that the Government can do, the best regard that it can show for young people, is to create an economic environment in which there is a growth in the number of jobs. The Government's policies have achieved this and they will continue to do so. The result of those policies is that there is now tremendous potential for job growth through the 1980s.
Of course, there is a major threat to this continued improvement in job opportunities. That major threat is the unrealistic attitude adopted by the trade union leadership and foisted on its members. This, of course, further demonstrates the hypocrisy of the honourable member for Wills, the former President of the Australian Council of Trade Unions, in raising this matter of public importance.
The improving employment trend evident over the last two years slowed down in July. The recent wave of industrial unrest was a major factor contributing to that short term slowdown in employment growth. It undoubtedly undermined the confidence of employers. Our potential for future job growth is jeopardised by the currency given to the economic madness in the proposition that each wage earner is entitled to the benefits of collective bargaining, work value wage hikes, consumer price index increases, productivity in-. creases and a shorter working week, all combined. To argue in this way is to raise the expectations of the Australian wage earner beyond what is an economic reality. Such thinking is a prescription

TARIFF PROPOSALS Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (4.27}-1 move:
Excise Tariff Proposals No.3 (1981).
Excise Tariff Proposals No. 3-1981-which 1 have just tabled formally place before Parliament alterations to the Excise Tariff. The alterations were introduced during the winter recess by Gazette Notice No. 1-1981-under the authority of section 160B of the Excise Act. Proposals No. 31981-increases the excise duty on stabilised crude petroleum oil from $174.08 to $177.53 per kilolitre operating on and from 1 July 1981. This alteration followed determination of new import parity prices by the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick). The determination accords with the Government's policy on import parity pricing for Australian produced crude oil. Proposals No. 3-1981---'-also increases the excise duty on naturally occurring liquefied petroleum gas from $36.67 to $37.07 per kilolitre operating on and from 1 July 1981. This change followed a new average wholesale price

for naturally occurring LPG being set by the Minister for National Development and Energy in accordance with Government policy. 1 commend the Proposals to the House.

On the face of it this seems to be at the heart of the Government's discredited import parity pricing program. We do not know whether there is any justification from what the Minister has just said. That is the first matter. Secondly, if 1 have interpreted it correctly, this proposal is just part of the import parity pricing program of this Government. EXPORT GRANTS ACTS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 20 August, on motion by MrAnthony:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (4.33)-Just to show my credibility I will say that the Opposition will not oppose the Export Grants Act Amendment Bill 1981. Nevertheless, I have some remarks to make, some of which are of a critical nature. I do not make them lightly. I believe there is some justification for suggesting that the theme of this Bill is best summed up in that well-known sentence: 'While Nero fiddles, Rome burns.' This Bill is part of Nero fiddling. The purpose of the Bill is to restructure the Export Development Grants Board which was set up under the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974. The restructuring is done in such a way that if this Bill is passed, as we anticipate it will be, the Chairman and the Deputy Chairman are drawn from the four part time members rather than the executive member being in the chair. The Board comprises four part time members and a full time member, making a total of five members who are responsible for the carrying out of the Board's decisions and the day to day operations of the secretariat.
Although the Board deals with a relatively large appropriation, estimated at $281m for this current financial year 1981-82, the changes which flow from the passing of this Bill are, as I have already indicated, very minor ones. They represent another change. This fiddling around is not at all justified in the second reading speech made by the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) in this chamber last week.
In this case, unlike the case of the tariff proposals with which I dealt, of course we have properly been given a week's notice in which to do our homework and look at the nature of this Bill. One can only reflect on the amount of time which is wasted on a little matter like this when there are so many great issues in this nation. When this House gets going each day we hear petitions being read. I believe those petitions are longer and more voluminous than they ever have been in the history of this House. We look at the Notice Paper and see the number of questions ori notice. I believe that the number is greater than it has ever

been. In my view this reflects the fact that the Government is governing the country in such a way that the people are so unhappy that the expression of that unhappiness is in this chamber, through petitions, through the questions on notice, and through the other matters such as the matter of public importance just recently raised in this House. There is no doubt that an enormous amount of hardship being suffered in this country could be focused on by legislation which is of a material nature. But instead we get this sort of fiddling around, making the chairman of the Board one of the part time members instead of the present situation in which the full time executive member is the chairman.
Let me mention some of the other problems we could deal with. We have 500,000 people unemployed. In the face of the increased interest rates, we have people worried sick trying to make ends meet. We have people Who are under enormous pressure because of the increased health charges, increased sales taxes and all these things that are worrying people in this community. Yet we are fiddling around changing a chairman from a full time member to a part time member. These changes were introduced last week, but the Act was established as long ago as 1978. I believe the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) will have a few words to say on the Bill. He is in the House and will follow me. He drew attention to what he described then as the piecemeal and fragmentary way in which the Government deals with this important segment of Australian industry, namely, our exporters. They deserve better than that. They deserve something better than just the piecemeal and fragmentary way in which they are dealt with. I shall talk in greater depth in a momentabout what they deserve.
In June 1980, the Australian Manufacturing Council appealed to the Government to bring some measure of certainty into this area. To date that appeal has been ignored. Whereas we could have had a Bill vastly improving the lot of the various exporters of this nation, instead, I repeat, we get this little machinery measure. Let us look at the Government's record in the area of exports. It is not a good one. In 1974-75, the Labor government established the basics of the present scheme, together with taxation concessions, and these amounted to over $LOOm per annum. Between 1976-77 and 1978-79, the Fraser Government wound the scherne down, first to a very low and quite unsatisfactory $25m a year level, and then it went back to $58m. One can see the stopping and going and the changes that are taking place instead of some consistent policy for exports. We

I am sure that honourable members agree that we deserve a little better than that. Indeed, we deserve something more certain than that and not to have everything left to the executive member in that open-ended way.
I end on this note: Where is the uniform code of reporting to cover the other 250 to 300 quasiautonomous national government organisations which, since the report of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations, we have learnt exist and about which this group of people who are so besotted about small government have had so much to say? When will we get some of the results of their thinking in relation to that Committee's report? Let us hear some arguments not only in favour of the recommendations arising out of that Senate' C'ommittee's report but also in support of the changes being made by this Bill, minor though they are. We have not been given the benefit of the Government's thinking as to why it is pursuing this matter. I believe governments must pay closer attention to the operations of boards such as this one. They must maintain their credibility with industry by seeing that boards and authorities are properly staffed and are subject to close scrutiny. I do not believe that this Bill does enough in that regard. If it does, we certainly have not been given an explanation of what is being done.
Nero fiddles and little changes like this are being made while so much more needs to be done for exporters. Our exports have been halved since 1953 on a percentage basis. We are now down to only 1.3 per cent of the world trade. It is an undoubted fact that our standard of living is closely related to our success as an exporter. Indeed, as our export performance has fallen, the standard of living has been relatively reduced in this country. At the time of Federation we were second or third in the world in terms of standard of living, whereas in 1970 we were twelfth on the league table. We are now down to No. 17 on that table. That has come at a time when our export performance has been declining. We have to do something about that. Of course, competitiveness is the key.
We cannot rely only on the energy equation going in our favour and on being a quarry for the rest of the world and exporting just those sorts of products. We must also get out and export manufactured products and do better with our agricultural products. We will not do that while competitiveness is being hit by theupvaluation of our currency. There has been an 11.3 per cent upvaluation since June of ,last year. There are

ways and means of making sure that our currency is not being upvalued at the expense of our exporters. We have to do more to specialise as well. Government must help industry to specialise so that it can get scale economies and can be more competitive on export markets, thus helping to bring about greater scale economies. We have to improve our transport system. Lastly, we have to encourage our own research and development and to build new export orders on Australian inventiveness and Australian ingenuity, and not just be happy with second hand technology. In short, the fiddling that is going on here is not proven because the Government has not given us the wisdom of its thoughts. I put that word in inverted commas with a query mark after it. The Opposition does not know enough to warrant its opposing this measure, but the Opposition takes this opportunity of saying that much more ought to be done by the Government to improve the lot of exporters so that they can export more of our products to the world markets.
Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (4.50)-1 support the Export Grants Acts Amendment Bill 1981, .which was brought into this House by the Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) ..We just listened to the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford). The honourable member certainly covered a wider field than the incentives scheme which this Bill deals with. One must agree with him that it is very important that we in Australia should do all we can to increase our exports. Whilst this Bill may not be a very large measure, it nevertheless provides certain important items which will improve our export incentives scheme.
The Commonwealth Government provides incentives to encourage exports. The Export Market Development Grants Act 197.. was introduced to operate for five years from July of that year, but it was revised to improve its effectiveness with effect from 1 July 1978. The scheme will now operate until 30 Jun,e 1982. The scheme is administered by the Export Development Grants Board, which is responsible to the Minister for Trade and Resources, and is designed to encourage exporters to seek out and develop overseas markets. The scheme covers the exporters of primary products and industrial goods and the providers of certain services, including tourism, know-how and industrial property rights, whether the exporter is an individual, a partnership, a company or a marketing organisation. The scheme operates by way of taxable grants to a

720 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Export Grants Acts Amenqment Bill

maximum of $100,000 and, in certain circumstances, $125,000 against eligible expenditure incurred on overseas market research and promotional publicity activities and development.
The Board also administers the Export Expansion Grants Act 1978, which provides for a scheme designed to reward improved export performance. Grants, which are taxable, are calculated on a formula applied to the increase in exports in a grant year over the average annual exports in the three years immediately preceding. The scheme covers a wide range of goods and services. However, some products are excluded. The products excluded under this scheme are minerals, wool, wheat, sugar, livestock, certain meats, WQodchips and hides and skins of cattle and sheep. The scheme took effect from 1 July 1977 and will run until mid-1982.
It has been mentioned by both the hQnourable member for Adelaide and me that it is important that we should do all we can to expand Qur exports. This is nQt easy tQday. FQr instance, we IQst valuable markets on the world scene when one Qf our major buyers, Great Britain, entered the European ECQnomic Community. We have lost possibly a sale of 80 tonnes of beef a year and sales Qf all other agricultural prQducts, including butter and dried fruits. This has had a very seriQus effect on our trade. It is good to. see that the Minister for Trade and ResQurces is meeting with the high Qfficials of the European Economic Co~ munity to. see what can be done to get AustralIa back into those valuable markets. NQt only is the EEC not purchasing our products but also the agricultural prQducts that it sells are heavily subsidised. This is causing great concern to marketers in Australia. .
Let us get back to the staff that are assisting the Board. On IQoking at some figures, we find that on 1 July 1979 the Board had a staff of 56 peQple. The staff ceiling, separate to. that for the Department of Trade and Resources, accorded to the Board for 30 June 1980 was initially set at 50. HQwever, following the Budget allQcations totalling $215m fQr the eXPQrt market development grant payments the ceiling was lifted to 80. I understand that, at the present time, it is slightly over this figure and that possibly further staff will be added in the near future. There are 29 staff members situated in Canberra, 20 in Sydney, 19 in Melbourne, four each in Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth and one staff member is situated in Hobart. There are part time assisting officers from the Department of Trade and Resources in Darwin.
The follQwing infQrmation will be' of great interest to. honourable members as it relates to

exports under the incentive schemes. FrQm 1974 to 1975 the total amQunt of claims determined were 13,975. Disallowed claims in full for that period totalled 1,073. The amount of claims totalled $187.3m and the total determined grants tQtalled $152.7m. In 1979-80, which was a big year for the incentive eXPQrt scheme, 3,507 claims were determined, 292 were disallowed and total claims amounted to $51.1m. Totalled determined grants amounted to ~4~.6m. ~his indi~ates that the incentive scheme IS IncreasIng and IS of great value to manufacturers and business people in Australia. MQst honourable members will have manufacturing concerns in their areas. No. dQubt there are concerns which have benefited greatly under the incentive scheme. In my electorate of Paterson, for instance, there are quite a number of manufacturers and concerns that have benefited in no. small way frQm the incentive grant scheme.
Mr Lloyd-You have helped them a IQt.
Mr O'KEEFE-That is right. We have helped them. Currently, the Board cQnsists Qf a full time chairman and fQur other part time members drawn from the Australian business community. This Bill is a machinery measure as has been mentioned by the honourable member for Adelaide. It provides fQr the restructing of the Export Developments Grants Board so that the chairman and deputy chairman are part time members of ~he Board with a full time member of the Board beIng responsible for carrying out the Board's decision and the day-tQ-day operations of the secretariat. This change will bring the Board into line with the structures of boards of other statutory bQdies which operate in this country.
There are 14 clauses in the Bill, some of which are minor in importance. However, I feel that clause 4 is an important clause. It provides fQr the structure and membership of the BQard and for the transfer of the person holding the office of chairman of the Board prior to the proclamation of this Bill to be deemed to have been appointed as the executive member upon prQclamation. Clause 5 transfers the term of office from the chairman of the Board to the newly created office Qf full time executive member. Clause 7 is important as it provides for the Minister to apPQint an acting executive member to the Board. Clause 8 provides fQr the selection of an acting chairman in the absence of the chairman and deputy chairman. Clause 9 outlines the duties of the chairman and of the executive member. These are important clauses. Although the Bill is a minQr one it does appear that most items affecting the staffing and machinery sections of the legislation. have been thoroughly dealt with.

It is a fact that, at the present time, our exports are not increasing as they should be. There are many reasons for this. We are having great difficulty on the eastern seaboard and, no doubt, in other ports in Australia, in getting our produce away. We are having great difficulty in particular with wheat and coal exports. One can go to the port of Newcastle which adjoins my electorate and one will find 30 or 40 ships off the coast which are held up because of go-slow tactics and oth~r union demands in that port. I am sure the situation is the same in other ports. The same situation applies to iron ore exports. These problems are affecting the export income of our country. The honourable member for Lyne (Mr Cowen) who flies over that port regularly will confirm the facts that I have just mentioned.
It is a tragedy that the export of our meat is under fire in the United States at the present time. The Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) is doing all that he possibly can to see that the culprits are caught and that this practice is eliminated. Not only is it affecting our .export trade to the United States but also it can affect our export of meat to Japan, Korea and to other markets which have been established throughout the world. This Export Grants Acts Amendment Bill will assist us in gaining increased export earnings for the business people and manufacturers of this country. It has the full support of honourable members on this side of the House.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (5.1)-1 wish to make a few remarks about the Export Grants Acts Amendment Bill. I do not oppose it. I took part in the debate when it was introduced. At that time. I was anxious to make the point that the whole idea of export incentive grants in this field are to be applauded where they guarantee an increase in employment because there would be an increase in productivity as a result of increased exports. Certain matters have been put to me which have caused me to make representations to the Government as to whether the administration of the legislation will be in accordance with what the House desires. I well remember that when we discussed this Bill we discussed it with a legislation committee-we were concerned as to how we might accurately draft the Bill to prevent people from getting benefits where there was no actual increase in exports. For example, would firms change their names and yet be the same firm?
Before I address myself to a couple of examples I make the point that when one looks at this year's Budget one will see some significant remarks. For example, in relation to the export expansion

grants scheme, this Budget provides $197m to reduce oustanding commitments of $247m. So that, as at 30 June 1981, we are already that far behind in oustanding commitments. That is how much that scheme has been delayed. If one looks at the export market development grants in the Budget one will see that, in similar terms, there is provision for an amount of $84m to reduce outstanding commitments of $1 09m. So there is an enormous backlog. I understand that other schemes in the world do not have this sort of delay. Obviously they are different schemes. I note that in the Industries Assistance Commission report it states that it will review this scheme. I think it ought to do that. The lAC was anxious to see that money was not held back from those who were entitled to it. Obviously the Budget has taken a guess at the situation because the demands have been so great that it cannot cope with the claims. In fact, there is a 12-month delay for those who are entitled to be paid.
I remind the House of some specifics that I think were canvassed when we discussed this matter in the legislation committee. The scheme provided for manufacturers, exporters and others to be given incentive grants. However, the question arose as to whether one might be put in a position of giving grants to people who were not, in fact, increasing their sales because they decided to sell the goods themselves or to have some other company do it. As I understand it these are some of the anomalies which have been pointed out to the Government. Let me take the case of a manufacturer selling a million dollars worth of goods through a merchant and selling direct half a million dollars worth of goods himself. The total sales are $1.5m. This scheme comes into operation so the manufacturer decides to dispense with the merchant. But, in fact, his total sales drop $300,000-they drop to $1.2m. But because he has sold them himself he gets the benefit of an incentive grant of $95,000. In summary, the situation is that although Australia loses $300,000 in sales, the manufacturer -gets from the taxpayer $95,000 under this scheme. Let us take another scheme. A manufacturer has been selling, completely through a merchant, $1.5m worth of goods. He sacks the merchant and sells the goods himself. But he sells less-only $lm worth. It can be seen from the figures that under the scheme he can claim $49,500 on the basis that there is an incentive for what he has done. I just do not think that that adds up to what we are about.
I have other examples here which show that an exporter can appoint a client as his agent and there can be some form of kick-back operation

Export Grants Acts Amendment Bill

where again, by manipulation of the arrangements, taxpayers' money is given for incentives which, in fact, have not occurred. A new exporter can come into the field offering rebates but creating no new business as he only takes away busi- 'ness from an existing exporter. For example, a million dollars worth of exports could be involved and a new exporter could come into the field offering a rebate. Honourable members can see that in those instances grants of $49,500 could be made when there has been no new business at all, just a change of name. If honourable members want to look at the present position regarding who is getting the benefits they will find that most of the major big business operations are certainly getting them but that in many cases they are foreign-owned and controlled and are exporting the Australian products back to their own corporations overseas and getting an export grant for it.
I really think we have to get some guidelines which indicate that we do not mind the concept of creating employment in Australia and that ought to be one of the criteria: We want to see Australian ownership and control of the business and we do not think it ought to apply right across the board to companies that are obviously exporting to themselves particularly our raw materials and matters of that nature. I think these examples have been drawn to the attention of the Government. I make the point that they represent a serious manipulation of the scheme. Is it any wonder that the scheme is so far behnd and that the present budgetary allowance is unable to cope with the demands of it?
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction and Minister Assisting the Minister for Trade and Resources) (5.8)-On behalf of the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony), I very sincerely thank all those honourable members who participated in the debate for their sound, constructive comments and for their concern. I would like briefly to respond to a few of the points made by the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford) and in particular to his comments about clause 9. The reason for clause 9 is that there has been a change in the structure of the Export Development Grants Board and it was necessary to indicate in the new legislation what would be the duties of the new officers-the Chairman and the executive member. It represents a rearrangement of functions as stated in the existing legislation and nothing has been changed or added.
The honourable member for Adelaide and the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) indicated their concern about some of the administrative problems that had

arisen. I will ensure that the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith receives a reply to the representations he has brought to the notice of the Government. I am unaware of the particular instances he brought to my notice, but we will endeavour to get him a positive and quick reply on those.
There have been administrative difficulties and each and everyone of us as a member of parliament has been made aware of those problems by our constitutents. These arrangements will ensure that some of those administrative problems and nightmares will be overcome. The legislation provides for the Chairman to undertake the work appropriate for a man in his position and for the executive member to concentrate on administration-an area where the Government is awa.re that problems and delays et cetera have arisen. I would advise all honourable members who are interested in the operations of the Export Development Grants Board that they are made clear to the Parliament in the annual report of the Board tabled by the Minister. In effect, the Government believes that restructuring the Board will make more effective use of the commercial and management expertise of the part time members in policy matters and, as stated, will allow the full time executive member to concentrate more on the administrative issues and the running of the secretariat.
The honourable member for Adelaide indicated some concern that the Government had not been definite in its undertakings to the Parliament and to the nation on this piece of legislation. I would like to rebut some of the arguments he made. The Government, when it reintroduced the scheme-I just remind the Parliament that the Labor Party did away' with the scheme in 1974-made it operative for five years. That was a definite commitment and I think the honourable member for Adelaide was not quite accurate when he alluded to Rome burning while Nero fiddled. As part of the Review of Commonwealth Functions decisions, the export incentive schemes have been extended to 30 June 1983 and the whole matter has been referred to the Industries Assistance Commission with a definite instruction to the lAC to report back to the Government by 31 December of this year, allowing the Government plenty of time to consider the report, to consult with exporters and to announce changes well in advance of the new arrangement coming into force: It appears to me that the Government has been quite definite. It indicated what it is going to do and the changed arrangements will lead to greater administrative efficiency.

SALES TAX AMENDMENT BILLS (Nos. lA TO 9A) 1981
Bills presented by Mr Howard, and read a first time.
Second Readings
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (5.15)-1 move:
That the Bills be now read a second time.
In my Budget Speech last week, I announced three separate sales tax changes. Two of them-an increase of 2t percentage points in existing rates and the correction of a number of anomalies and inconsistencies that have crept into

the sales tax law-are already before the Parliament in legislative form. The BiBs I am now introducing complete the legislative package and will, with effect from I January next, impose sales tax of 2t per cent on a range of goods that are now exempt from the tax.
In speaking last week, I drew attention to the clear advantage that lies in effecting some change in the mix of direct and indirect taxes. I spoke about and again emphasise the potential that such a change generates for desirable reductions in direct taxes, particularly personal income tax, as economic and budgetary conditions permit. It is said by some that the Government's Budget decision is at variance with what I put to the House in my taxation statement in March this year. Nothing could be further from the truth, as a fair analysis of that statement and a consideration of the modest change now proposed will reveal.
As honourable members know, it is, for constitutional reasons, established practice to enact nine separate Acts when imposing sales tax. The approach in each of the present nine Bills is to impose tax of 2+ per cent on goods that are identified by reference to items in the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act that at present exempt the goods from tax. These exempting items, along with a large number of other exemptions that are being retained, are contained in the First Schedule to the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act.
The technical effect of the Bills will be to tax at 2t per cent, on and after I January 1982, goods which are at present exempted by items 51, 53, 54, 58,60,82, to 90F inclusive, 96, lOS, 107, 113F, 120 and l20A in the first Schedule. The broad classes of goods which fall within the scope of the items other than 107 and 113F are books, magazines and newspapers, building materials, packaging and wrapping materials, hand tools, clothing, footwear, drapery, soft furnishings and manchester goods. The goods covered by items 107 and 113F are, very broadly, motion picture films, servicing equipment and construction equipment. I emphasise that goods falling within any of these categories will still be exempt from sales tax if they are sold in circumstances in which an overriding exemption in the First Schedule to the Sales Tax (Exemptions and classifications) Act applies. By way of example, where a school entitled to exemption under item 63A purchases books for the school's use, the books will remain exempt, from sales tax. Similarly, where a manufacturer purchases hand tools for use as aids to manufac. ture, as defined, the tools will remain exempt under the aids to manufacture head of exemption.

724 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Norfolk Island Amendmel1t Bill

I shall not go into greater detail in this connection at this stage as an explanatory memorandum covering technical aspects of the Bills is being made available to honourable members and this provides details of the goods affected by these measures. The net result of the changes will be an increase in revenue estimated at $132m in 1981-82 and $330m in a full year.
Changes in sales tax are a significant part of this year's Budget; indeed, they are an integral part of it. As I indicated earlier, the present Bills complete the legislative f~amework for the changes and it is desirable that they soon become law. I therefore trust that the Parliament will give these and the earlier Bills a speedy passage. In that spirit, I commend the present Bills-the Sales Tax Amendment Bills (Nos. IA to 9A) 1981-to the House.

NORFOLK ISLAND AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 20 August, on motion by MrWilson:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (5.20)-The Opposition does not oppose this measure. It is designed to change the structure of the Norfolk Island Supreme Court in order to permit the appointment of a justice and to bring into line the rules of appointment of justices with those which apply to other Federal courts in respect of retirement"and the removal of a judge from another Federal court. It is not' a measure which should delay the House long. It would provide an opportunity to discuss some arrangements for Norfolk Island but I do not think it is appropriate at this time. I raise only one matter, and that concerns the appointment of justices to serve in part time positions in this area. It may well be that the Government should look at the capabilities of thejudges in carrying out that function given their workloads arid their ages. The two judges on Norfolk Island at the moment seem to fit one into each of those categories.
Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (5.21)-1 welcome and support this BilL It is, of course, an amendment of the principal Act, the Norfolk Island Act 1979, which is the basic framework for the introduction of self-government on Norfolk Island and which provides for the judicial

system-that is, the part of the Act which is now being amended-and the new role of the Administrator, the Executive Council and tbe Legislative Assembly. The Act divides sovereignty between the Commonwealth of Australia and the Legislative Assembly of Norfolk Island. I think it can be said that it has worked extremely well despite some difficulties since it was introduced. It schedules various powers to be administered by the Legislative Assembly while th~ Commonwealth retains other powers. It is part of the new political and economic surge in the Territory of Norfolk ~sland. It takes its place with other measures such as the Commonwealth's decision to upgrade-on the recommendation of the Public Works Committee, the airpoit on the island, the adoption by the Legislative Assembly of its flag, the decision to examine systematically the Public Service on the island and a range of other measures.
It is stated in the preamble to the Act that, within a period of five years after the coming into operation of the Act, consideration will be given to extending the powers conferred by or under it on the Legislative Assembly. Accordingly, we can expect that consideration to lead possibly to significant and major changes. Meanwhile, this Bill seeks to appoint a chief judge on the island. The Supreme Court of Norfolk Island is the Supreme Court of record for the Territory. It is the apex of Norfolk Island law. There is an appeal from it to the Federal Court of Australia and in certain circumstances to the High Court of Australia with special leave of that court. Below the Supreme Court is the Court of Petty Sessions which consists of six laymen. It is, of course, a far busier court. According to the last annual report, it dealt with 80 cases in its civil jurisdiction and 80 cases in its criminal jurisdiction -whereas the Supreme Court dealt with two cases in its civil jurisdiction and two in its criminal jurisdiction. Whilst it is not as busy as the Court of Petty Sessions it deals with issues on a legally higher level and is manned-if one may use that expression-by distinguished lawyers. At the moment it has two justices, Mr Justice Dunphy and Mr Justice Evatt, but there is no chief judge.
This legislation aims to bring the Supreme Court of Norfolk Island into line with the corresponding court in the other territories of Australia. That is a significant step forward. It is a measure which I know will be welcomed by most people on Norfolk Island. The other matters dealt with in the legislation-for example, the retiring age and the termination of an appointment in certain circumstimcesc-are also common sense

Apple and Pear Corporation Bill (No.2)

measures that will be welcomed. For these reasons, I also welcome the legislation.

Mr WILSON (Bonython-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House has a general debate covering this Bill, the Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Bill and the Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Bill, as they are associated measures. Separate 9uestions will, of course, be put on each of the BIlls at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Jarman)-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these three measures? I will allow that course to be followed.

and there is no need for me to go into the Bills themselves.
As honourable members know, this is an off year in the bi-annual bearing cycle for the 1982 crop and it is expected to result in fewer apples being available for the fresh domestic and export markets. Consequently, domestic fresh prices are likely to rise to around$450 a tonne, while average export returns are estimated to be. only marginaly higher than the $290 a tonne estImated for the 1981 season. Processing prices are predicted to rise by about 5 per cent to average $90 to $95 a tonne, reflecting the higher fresh prices and increased demand for processing apples. Total Australian production this year is estimated to be around 300 kilotonnes, assuming average off year yields. Bearing tree numbers in Australia are stabilising at around 5.3 million trees.

Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (5.26)-On the face of it these apple and pear Bills are trivial and innocu-
ous but, of course, they do relate to .an industry with which quite a few members of thIS House are
very much concerned. It is a regional industry. ~t is an industry which is getting back to domestIc consumption with not much export. As a former apple grower and fruit grower, I take a fair amount of interest in this industry. Recently I visited various fruit growing areas in South Australia, Queensland and New South Wales. The purpose of the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Bill (No.2) is to change the number of grower members of the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation from four to six. I do not think that is very controversial and I do not expect the Australian Democrats to attempt to amend the Bill by saying that the number should be five. The industry for sometime has been lobbying the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) to increase the number of grower representatives to take into account .that the nonexport States were not recognised on the Apple and Pear Corporation. The preSsure came particularly from South Australia ,and Queensland.

Decreased availability of apples will result in about 180 kilotonnes going to the domestic fresh market, a fall of about 15 per cent. Total intake of apples for processing in 1982 is f~recast to ~e around 75 kilotonnes, 7 per cent hIgher than 10 1981. It is anticipated that stocks of apple juice concentrate will decline to more normal levels by the beginning of the 1982 season and exports of fresh apples for the 1982 season are estimated to be about 45 kilotonnes, approximately 25 per cent lower than the 1981 estimates. While these exports will face competition on European mar- . kets from locally produced fruit and other southern hemisphere produce, lower supplies of Australian fruit and better quality fruit may help to increase returns in 1982. The expected high returns on all markets will not offset the effects of the forecast decline in production, with the result that the total gross value of production may be down 2 per cent to around $104m.
The apple and pear industry is worth roughly $160m, and the balance is taken up by the pear industry. The availability of pears relates to some degree, particularly in the Goulburn Valley, to

726 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Apple and Pear Corporation Bill (No.2)

the overflow from what is canned. We need to take that into account. As many honourable members would know, the problem in the deciduous fruit canning industry, particularly the pear canning industry, is to be subject to quite a lot of discussion in the coming years as the European market substantially winds down.
I do not think there is much else I can say about this industry. The few words I have said relate to the outlook for the industry. I think people within the industry realise that they are going to have to look more and more to the domestic market and at the sorts of arrangements which take place there. The people within the industry are not all optimistic or sanguine about what is to happen and quite a few of them are being constructively critical. They say that the decline in exports that has been experienced over the past decade and the evidence of a fall-off of sales in Australia give cause for concern, because there is also a significant reduction in processing requirements. Yet there is no overall industry plan for the future producers. That is a rather dismal outlook for the industry in the 1980s.
I suppose it is up to the apple and pear growers themselves to steer their industry towards more prosperity. To do so they need to support an indepth examination of a number of issues. They must agree on sound objectives and planning which will achieve such objectives. It is not my place to be critical of the industry, but I can reflect some of the concerns of people within the industry who are concerned about irregular cropping and quality matters. Many people in the industry are concerned about industry statistics, and it is quite certain that accurate industry statistics are a necessary ingredient for industry viability. Quite frankly, the current methods used to collect statistical information are archaic, ineffective and of little real value. Often the information produced in the various apple growing areas is rather misleading or obsolete. As I said, with a production. value annually of approximately $160m and little in the way of basic, accurate information on which to plan, it is to be expected that the industry should flounder. An entirely new approach through producer organisations, guaranteeing to preserve grower anonymity, is long overdue just in this area of the collection of statistics. I suppose that, had priorities to be allocated for problem areas within this increasingly domestic industry, quality would command the top position, because no marketing system, no promotional campaign and no industry . plan or price reduction will ever substitute for quality. Earlier this year, Mr Bruce Lockier, the

Manager of the Apple and Pear Growers Association of South Australia, said:
There can be no denial that over the past few years the general overall quality of apples and pears has deteriorated. Emphasis has been, and is still being placed on the appearance of the fruit rather than condition. This has proved to be an extemely expensive exercise for the industry.
One significant contributing factor to inferior quality could be the decline in export which has forced many growers to change a life-long op()ration of growing, picking, packing and exporting to one of growing, picking, storing and marketing. This could involve varietal changes and certainly necessitates an in-depth knowledge of market requirements and the production and storage of fruit with a long.shelf life.
Top priority must be given to this great problem if the industry is to continue to prosper. Perhaps the employment of a' quality control officer by the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation should be considered. Another area in which there has been some debate is market research. An industry with such annual production and capital investment really does need some form of regular market research conducted by professional agencies. It is accepted that market research has played a major role in the establishment, success and progress of the food chains and should be used by the apple and pear industry forthwith initially to identify distribution efficiencies, changes in consumer preferences and weakness in sales outlets and generally to improve the effectiveness of publicity campaigns.
I will talk on only one other matter. Plastic returnable containers are a comparatively new innovation in the industry but are regarded by the growers who are using them as the greatest single benefit to the industry since-the introduction of bulk handling. Recently in Adelaide I saw the distribution centre. While not a problem, it is perhaps very appropriate that a warning should be sounded that unless a siugle handling system is agreed upon very soon and such agreement is supported by governments, a multiplicity of containers will emerge and a costly, chaotic situation will eventuate. In spite of the continuing decline in exports of apples and pears the word 'export' appears to have some sort of magical appeal to many people within the industry. Its future no doubt is questionable and the amount of money spent each year in the entire export area of apples and pears should be matched with its overall value to the industry. The amount being devoted to promotion is a matter that will cause more and more concern with respect to the stabilisation plan.
Let me briefly summarise what I have said. The industry must realise the potential of Australian markets and the urgent need to correct the components that are currently impeding an expansion

(No.2) is to increase the size of the Corporation

from four to six to allow for two more grower

members, one from each State, and to increase the

maximum rate allowed by the legislation for levies

and charges. The Minister for Primary Industry

(Mr Nixon) indicated in his second reading

speech that he had responded to the request of the

Apple and Pear Growers Association to allow

grower representation from each State. The argu-

ments used are that as 80 per cent of the apple

crop is sold locally it is important to have rep-

resentation from 'each State and in this way also

increase the understanding, through the grower

organisation and back to the growers, of the work

of the Corporation.

-

I wish to express my concern at this increase in the grower membership from four to six. I hope that it is not the start of a general and what I consider to be regressive trend to have grower membership on a State basis. It does not show any
reflection of the size of the industry in each State. The rural industry information papers indicate that there is a substantial difference in the size of the apple industry, let alone the pear industry, between the States. I believe that the legislation will increase the size of a statutory authority beyond what is sensible. I believe also that it will tend to make grower representatives, think of themselves as representing a State rather than the industry as a whole nationally. (Quorum formed). As I was about to say before I was interrupted, I hope that this increase in the membership of the Apple and Pear Corporation to allow grower representation from each State is not the start of a new trend. I would consider'such a trend to be regressive and to be a tendency away from representatives considering they are representing the industry nationally instead of just each State. I am far happier to support the second major amendment which increases the maximum permissible levy. In fact, it more than doubles it because the maximum levy charged has not been increased since 1976. I believe that carries with it a very important principle of accountability to the Corporation
and the grower members and organisations

associated with it, that is that it is for them to decide what the level of that levy or charge should be and then to justify it to their grower members.
I wish to also draw the attention of people to a few facts about the scale of the apple and pear industry in this country. Let us look at the production of fresh apples and pears in this nation. Victoria is the leading State in that industry.
Mr Good luck-No.
Mr Hodgman-No.
Mr LLOYD-Victoria is easily the leading State in the production of fresh apples and pears. I refer to the rural industry informatiom papers. In 1979-80 Victoria was the leading apple producing State. Let us get a few facts iilto this business. I acknowledge that in most years Tasmania is the , leading apple producing State, but not by very much. If we take apples and pears together Victoria is easily the major producing State. If we take apples and pears together the electorate of Murray out-produces every State in Australia, including Tasmania. I am saying this in a kindly manner, Mr Deputy Speaker, so we can get some perspective Into the whole debate. Victoria produces 100,000 tonnes out of a possible production of 127,000 tonnes nationally. One can continue to cite figures from rural industry information papers.
Mr Hodgman-Talk about quality.
Mr LLOYD-If we are to talk about quality, the pears from Victoria sell far better overseas than the apples from Tasmania. So we will not go down that track either. Let me continue with~' establishing a few facts in this debate. I do so in a kindly manner. If one looks at the number of fruit growers in relation to the industry, then the Murray electorate has more than Tasmania or any of the other States of Australia. If one looks at the scale of the fresh pear export industry, it is usually about half to two-thirds of the apples that are exported. This raises a far more fundamental question of imbalance in the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation than has been addressed by the amendments to this legislation; that is, having a grower representative from each State. With almost every situation there will be representatives of the apple industry. There is a far greater imbalance in relation to the pear and apple industry between those who represent the apple sector and those who represent the pear sector. Apple and Pear Corporation Bill (No.2)

by what it considers to be the bias to the apple industry rather than the total apple-and pear industry in membership in the Apple and Pear Corporation. The unhappiness is increased by these amendments.

I also draw the attention of the Parliament to the disastrous situation that faces the fresh apple and pear export industry this season. The industry is now in grave financial difficulty. The recently enacted underwriting stabilisation scheme will obviously be fully utilised and the Minister and the Government are to be commended for its introduction. Some of the growers believe that the scale is not adequate. I understand that the Minister said to them: 'We have to allow the scheme to work its way through'-I agree with that-'and then have a look at the situation'.

I will quote a few of the prices to indicate the disastrous situation the exporters face this season. For the first week in May 1980 Victorian packham pears were being sold on the London market at $22.88 a box. In 1981 the price had dropped to $19.49. By the first week in July this year, compared to 1980 when the price was $24.34, the price was down to $11.62 and it dropped even further in Hamburg to about $7 a box.-The price for apples in 1980 was $19 a box and it had halved to $9 this year. One of the major reasons for this disastrous selling season is the ap-
preciation in the value of the Australian dollar. One has to admit that there is a carry-over and a very large increase in exports from the southern
hemisphere, et cetera. One has to acknowledge the fact and draw it to the attention of parliamen-
tarians and people throughout this nation that this export industry and others related to it, such as the canned fruit industry, are suffering and have very great difficulties because of the appreCiation of the Australian dollar. I support the legislation.
