APPROPRIATION BILL (No.1) 1981-82
Second Reading
(Budget Debate)
Debate resumed from 18 August, on motion by MrHoward:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (8.0)-Mr Speaker, the Budget of any national government is among many things but more impo.rtantly an annual allocation of financial resources between competing community interests. In its simplest form, it is a sharing of the national cake each year between who gets what, and who has to pay. For six years now, this Government has used the Budget to distort persistently and grossly the distribution of people's taxes. At the same time, the aggregate of those taxes has reached staggering proportions. Privileged minority interests, specifically big business, have been the major beneficiaries. The Australian people, as individuals and as a nation, have been the losers. And the greatest losers have been the disadvantaged, the needy, and the less well-off. This Budget from this Government-its eighth Budget in six years-is perhaps the worst example of this insidious and threatening process. I say 'threatening', and I mean 'threatening'.
To describe t~is Budget as reasonable, fair or responsible is to prostitute the English language. It is none of these things. Mr Eric Risstrom 9f the

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 731

Australian Taxpayers Association, a man of impeccable objectivity and credibility, has called it the worst Budget in 34 years. It ignores the needs of the average family. It dismisses the desperation of the disadvantaged. It persists with a callous indifference to people's security, their livelihood,. their homes, their spending power,. and their future. It threlltens the very existence of some three million Australians whom even the Government now recognise as living in poverty.
I repeat: three million Australians-one in every five of our people-live a hand to mouth, day to day existence, often in appalling conditions, in what is inherently the wealthiest country in the world, the so-called lucky country. The reality of this paradox is a national disgrace. We have exploited migrant Australians who live in over-crowded urban ghettos. We have unemployed young Australians who have not been able to find a job in four years. We have pensioner Australians who eat pet food to stay alive. We have black Australians, deprived and ignored, who must believe the so-called resources development boom is a sick joke.
All these people hear their Government brag at election time about the prosperity that is just around the corner, and then ten months later they have their immediate future decided by a Budget like this-a Budget that attacks everyone'li living standards, but which shames and humiliates those most in need in our community.
This Government, by its rigid disregard over six years for the interests of all Australians, is totally responsible for these circumstances. And no-one is more responsible than the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). This morning I sent the Prime Minister a copy of an article from yesterday's issue of the Sydney Morning Herald to read while he recuperates from his illness, a recuperation, which I trust will be speedy. It was the first of three articles on poverty in Australia, and it should be required reading for all members opposite, particularly the Treasurer (Mr Howard). I defy any member of the Government to read this shattering article by Mr Graham Williams, and then to go home and sleep confortably tonight. To make my point, I intend to quote briefly from the article. Mr Williams describes a conversation with a 15-year-old Sydney girl who has.not worked for nine months and who says she has nothing to live for. He then goes on to write:
I could have told her of the nine-year"old boy who for weeks took a block of wood wrapped in paper to his innerwestern suburbs school so his classmates wouldn't know his mother could not afford to give him a sandwich.

I could have told her of the many lone parents, crippled by their tiny pension and their big rent bill, who go for days without eating so their children can have food.
I could have told her of Sydney's 15,000 homeless who nightly herd into doss-houses, or sleep in parks, on station platforms, or in friends' flats.
Or the 14-year-old boy who recently created the dubious record of being the youngest to doss down at the Matthew Talbot Hostel for homeless men at Woolloomooloo, along with 480 men.
Those few paragraphs say more about what is happening to Australia today after almost six years of Fraser Government than anything uncovered by the barren and sterile prose of the Treasurer's Budget Speech. They suggest a harsh reality that has absolutely nothing in common with the fantasy of the Prime Minister's election policy speech just ten months ago in which he said:
Today, a new spirit, a new enthusiasm, a new dynamism, is at work in Australia. And while achieving all this, we have enhanced the dignity and self-esteem of those in need.
Mr Graham Williams did not find much dignity and self-esteem among the people he interviewed on the growing disaster of poverty in this country. And neither did a sub-committee of the World Council of Churches which visited Australia two months ago, and then reported earlier this month.
It stated:
All the good intentions contained in programs which treat Aboriginal people as the objects of charity and paternalism, are dwarfed by an over-riding reality of massive deprivation, poverty and injustice.
Aborigines have become the invisible, unseen and unheaTd people of. Australia. They neither share in the prosperity nor the promise of a free, democratic and affluent Australian nation.
These examples I have quoted suggest a massive indifference by the Prime Minister either to. the truth or the brutal harshness of his Government's economic policies. Either he will say anything at election time., or he is ignorant of the real suffering and hardship his policies inflict on so many Australians. Perhaps he prefers to double-indemnify himself by doing both. The real fault lies, I believe, in his isolation from reality. The Prime Minister is cocooned in a comfortable lifestYle sustained by a $100,000 salary and a $1 in farm property that effectively ensures he has nothing in common with 98 per cent of his 15 million fellow Australians. The Prime Minister is as remote from all the problems of getting a home loan, or finding a job, or making the average pay packet stretch from one week to the next; or adequately feeding and clothing a family, as he is from the man on the moon.
These week to week anxieties, which literally hundreds of thousands of Australian families now face all the time, simply have no real meaning for

732 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

the Prime Minister or indeed for most of his Cabinet. And] doubt that the Prime Minister has ever been anywhere near such a place as the Matthew Talbot doss-house for homeless men, or an overcrowded inner-city ghetto, or a run-down Housing Commission apartment block, or an Aboriginal shanty town in Western New South Wales or Northern Queensland. Yet this is the Prime Minister who, for six years, has presided over policies which perpetuate a system which takes most from those who can least afford to give, and gives most to. those with little need at all. Over the last five years, the Fraser Government's economic policies have taken $1 24,000m in taxes from the people of Australia. Over the next 12 months, this Government proposes to take another $37,768m in taxes from the same people. In total, some $162,000m in taxes in six years. All of it taken from the Australian people. All of it in taxes. And all of it collected by the highest taxing Government in our history. I remind the House that this is the Government that came into office promising tax reforms and tax cuts. Yet this is the Government now responsible for our highest taxes ever.

Three times the Prime Minister bribed the

people of this country at election time with prom-

ises of lower taxes. Each time, after the elections

had been won, the Prime Minister promptly

wiped out his promise with increased taxes. Tax

indexation was promised in 1975, introduced in

1976, then progressively phased out, partially

reintroduced, and now eliminated totally, A tax

cut was promised in 1977 and then, after an early

election, it was eliminated by the introduction of a

tax surcharge in 1978. In 1979, the Fraser

Government ended the surcharge and called the

decision tax reform. Last year, foul' of every fiye

taxpayers were given a miserable 90c a week tax

benefit. They were also promised half tax indexa-

tion from 1 July this year. Now, with the elections

once more behind him, the Prime Minis.ter has

again abandoned tax indexation and increased

taxes right

yet again, across the

bthoiasrdt.imIteiswaitshtahgiggehreirngsarleecsotradxeosf

duplicity and dishonesty: I have no doubt that the

same formula will be tried again before the next

elections in two years' time.

This Budget, in a nutshell, is a case of slug them now and try to bribe them later. The Prime Minister has a simple faith in the principle that he can keep fooling most of the people most of the time. But the damage has been done and it will not be forgotten. Australians have been hurt too viciously ever to trust this Government again. The Prime Minister has no credibility left on taxation. I repeat that the tax grab by the Fraser Government in six years is $162 billion. Just on $90 billion

of this is income tax. Another $11 billion has. been plundered from motorists under the Government's petrol taxing policies. It has ruthlessly exploited overseas oil prices to squeeze every dollar possible from Australian motorists in taxing petrol which mostly comes from our own crude oil in Bass Strait. This year, the petrol tax will reap the Government another $3,440m-an increase of well over 1,000 per centin six years. The increase in income tax revenue this year is just as regressive. It will go up a massive 22 per cent, or $3,349m. This represents, on average, an extra $11 a week paid by every taxpayer in this country. In fact, the Fraser Government this year will collect more in income tax alone than .the Whitlam Government collected in all taxes in its last year of office. This Government now proposes to raise almost as much tax from all sources in a single year as the Whitlam Government raised in three years.
So much for the Prime Minister's persistent fiction of his Government being a low tax government. It is not a low tax government, and never has been. It is responsible for the highest taxes in this nation's history. Even worse, it has completely reversed government spending patterns in the six years it has been in power. Table 4 on Budget outlays in the Treasurer's Speech sets out how the Government is spending the record taxes it has ripped off Australians since it came to office. It shows not only that wage and salary earners are paying more taxes than ever, but also that far less of these funds are going back to the community to benefit people directly. Six years ago, under a Labor government, our major spending priorities were health, education, housing, welfare, and urban and regional development. These priorities have been turned upside down by the Fraser Government. Compared with six years ago, and after allowing for inflation, the major increases in government spending this year are: Parliamentary expenditure-up 65 per cent; assistance to industry-up 56 per cent; police, security, and detention-up 52 per cent; and law, order, and public safety-up 34 per cent. These are this Government's priorities-the self-indulgence of Parliament, the transfer of government resources to business and industry, and the Prime Minister's fetish with security and law and order.
At the other end of the scale come all the major programs to assist people which six years ago were the priorities of a Labor government. Spending in real terms on education has fallen 2 per cent since 1975; health is down 22 per cent; transport and communications are down 53 per cent; water supply, electricity and gas are down 57 per cent

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 733

and housing is down 59 per cent. At the very bottom of the list is government spending on urban and regional development, and the environment. That has been slashed 89 per cent in real terms in six years. What this means is that big government is alive arid flourishing more than ever. All that has changed is the Government's division of the cake. High and rising interest rates have pushed savings bank home loans out of the reach of four out of every five families seeking their first home. Yet Government spending on housing has plummeted just under 60 per cent in six years. At the same time, direct assistance to industry in grants, subsidies and tax concessions goes up 56 per cent in real terms to just under $1 ,OOOm.
The Government cannot find $9m to continue funding its modest programs for unemployed young people under the Community Youth Support Scheme-a scheme that directly employs some 700 mostly full time staff and last year aided 52,000 out of work youth. But it has no problem allocating more than $22m to fund the operations of the Australian Security Intellience Organisation, not Ic of which is publicly accountable. In six years under the Fraser Government, ASIO's budget has shot up 100 per cent in real terms, with never an explanation why publicly and, I can assure honourable members, never a justification why privately. The same sort of intimidating security mentality now pervades this building, which has come to resemble more a military bunker than the people's Parliament. The Australian Federal Police, according to the Treasurer, will spend more on incidental expenditure than it will cost for a year's unemployment benefits for 1,000 out of work teenagers. They are the standards of the Fraser Government. They are the sorts of priorities it employs in taxing people at record levels while at the same time cutting back on those programs which directly benefit the community.
There is some assistance in higher family allowances for one family in every four, but nothing for I t million families with two children or fewer. There is $62m being spent this year on limited family allowances, but an extra $550m is being taken back from all families in the application of the .new sales tax increases. The Opposition opposes those tax measures and will fight them vigorou~ly in both Houses. The sales tax proposals are unfair and highly inflationary. Four months ago the Treasurer promised there would be no such broadly based indirect tax without an offsetting reduction in income tax. Now the Government is trying to sneak them into operation in defiance of its promise and without any mandate from the people. The Prime Minister's empty

threats will not change our opposition to these taxes. That opposition will be implacable. They must be rejected.
So too, at the first opportunity, must the Fraser Government's attitude to Aboriginal welfare be reversed. For six years the Prime Minister has postured as a supporter of Aboriginal welfare. He is now monumentally embarrassed by the World Council of Churches report on the eve of the Commonwealth talk fest in Melbourne next month. He should be no less embarrassed by his Government's own spending programs on Aboriginal welfare over the last six years. In real terms, spending has fallen 35 per cent since the last Labor Budget. If the Prime Minister honoured his election promise to maintain existing levels of support programs for Aborigines, this Government would be in debt to the Aboriginal people for $213m-the cumulative loss of funds in real terms over six years. Aboriginal housing programs are down, in real terms, by 43 per cent, education funding is down 30 per cent, and health funding is down 22 per cent.
Another example of the Government's erratic sense of priority is in the defence vote. It has been turned up and down over the years as domestic political factors have dictated. In 1976, the Government committed itself to a five year equipment purchase plan calling for real annual increases of 5 per cent. In fact, over the four years to 1979-80, the increase averaged only 3.9 per cent. In 1980, in a knee jerk reaction to the Russiap. invasion of Afghanistan, a new plan was stitched up calling for annual increases in spending of 7 per cent. In the event, the -last Budget managed to produce an increase of 4.8 per cent and this Budget envisages an increase of 5.3 per cent.
The effect of these shortfalls will be twofold. First, the log-jam of purchasing commitments over the next few years will put an enormous and perhaps impossible strain on our resources. To meet both firm and reasonably firm commitments the Government, by the end of this financial year, will be facing a bill of more than $5 billion. The second effect of this procrastination will be to aggravate the confusion already created in defence planning by the Government's ambivalent and cynical policies.
This Budget means more inflation, even higher interest rates, less economic growth, no joy for the unemployed, and more taxes. It is another savage attack on the general living standards of the Aus~ tralian family. Six years ago, Australians were promised sustained economic prosperity. Last

734 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

year, for the first time, there was some firm indication that this prosperity was returning. It happened despite all the best intentions of the Treasurer and the Government to prevent the economic conditions that created it. Higher wages, strongly opposed by the Government, lifted the spending power of family pay packets. This fuelled consumer spending and contributed two-thirds of growth in gross domestic product for the year. A year ago, the Government argued that strong wages growth would destroy economic growth. In fact, the reverse occurred, as the Labor Party argued it would.
Despite these indicators, the economy slowed down after Christmas. It slowed down because the brakes were applied to both wages growth and consumer spending, the two ingredients that had most helped a lift in the economy. At the same time, there was a marked easing in the housing industry and employment growth tailed off. Nonfarm product growth slumped from an annual rate of 6.1 per cent in the first half of 1980-81 to only 1.6 per cent in the second half. However, the Department of the Treasury ignored the renewed signs of another slide. It recognised that the Government prefers the sterility of its own primitive myths rather than learn the lessons of recent history. This Budget puts the economic gear lever firmly back into reverse. There will be less spending and therefore less economic activity. The wages bill is forecast to rise by more than 15 per cent, but income tax will increase much faster at 22 per cent.
Economic growth will be hauled back because money supply is set to increase 10.5 per cent but nominal growth in'the economy by 14.5 per cent, a shortfall in money supply of at least 4 per c.ent on community needs. That will certainly be more contractionary than the Government's dubious budget arithmetic admits. Costs will be higher because of high and rising interests rates. Already in the last 16 months higher interest rates will have added more than $1,OOOm to the interest bill alone on home mortgages. This is money taken away from family consumer spending-money that cannot be afforded by the home buyer, or, indeed, by the economy.
Australia now has the dubious distinction of having the second highest real rate of interest in the world, just marginally behind that of the United States. Australia business will pass these charges on to consumers, and housing costs will be pushed even further beyond the reach of despondent families. Interest rates must stay high while ever the Government continues to encourage an even larger inflow of foreign money than the extraordinary level of $6.5 billion reached last

year. More than 75 per cent of last year's inflow in the first nine months was largely speculative investment in real estate, shares and the takeover of established Australian industry. The Government accomlllodated this rampant speculation simply by cutting back on the spending power and community security of Australian families by taxing away their living standards with high interest rate increases. This processis now set to repeat itself, along with further revaluations of the Australian dollar to the disadvantage of primary producers; exporters and import replacement industries..
The forecast flood of foreign money into Australia this year, by the Government's own estimate, is counted at some $7 billion. Rather than impose immediate controls, the Government has sought to offset the impact on money supply by savage monetary policy measures and cutbacks in public sector spending through massive increases in family taxation. Thus the price of this Government's failure to limit foreign capital inflows is the proposed 22 per cent increase in income tax and the 39 per cent lift in sales tax. In short, last financial year concluded with the Australian economy winding down rapidly because of the severity of Government monetary policy. It was not a case of an over-heated economy, but of one clearly running out of steam. The contractionary impact of this latest Budget will only accelerate the run-down.
For the last four years, we have urged this Government to adopt controlled expansionary policies to get the economy moving and to sustain recovery. Each time, the Government has acted either timidly or extremely harshly. Each time it has been wrong. Its Budget forecasts have been wrong and its promises have not been met. The resultant measures, particularly in the field of taxation, have been even more restrictive and punitive on the incomes of the average family. Now it has acted completely contrary to the direction of most economic indicators.
Let me suggest what is needed. A balanced domestic budget, or even a mild deficit, is what is required at this time. Certainly, the economy can afford it and it would be consistent with falling inflation, lower interest rates, and sustained growth of 5 per cent, provided controls are imposed immediately on short term speculative money from overseas. That is, We are' talking about making domestic economic activity by Australians by allowing money to be made available to them rather than foreign carpetbaggers speculating in real estate, shares and in Australian industry to the qetriment of Australian's welfare and security.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 735

We would immediately abandon the proposed sales tax increases. This would leave $550m in family pay packets to go into consumer spending. Ten months ago, in the elections, we proposed an across the board cut in income tax. A more
generous reduction now could not be opposed on economic or moral grounds. Given the massive 22 per cent increase in income tax revenue the
Government is forecasting this year, that reduction is easily accommodable. We would also introduce a resource rent tax on mining
companies who earn excess profits. We would immediately get rid of the $500m investment allowance to industry which continues to replace jobs with machines. Both moves would begin the process of redirecting resources back from a bouyant corporate sector into public programs that assist people, particularly housing, education, job creation, urban and regional development, and Aboriginal welfare.

I repeat that we are committed to a universal health insurance scheme based on each family's
ability to pay. Basic medical and hospital insurance will, in a week's time under the Government's latest health policy switch, cost the aver-
age family $10 a week. Six years ago the same basic cover was provided as a right from general revenue. Now it is a direct tax on families, costing an estimated $1200m a year. However, instead of .paying the tax to the Fraser Government the Fraser Government has arranged that families will pay it direct to the private health funds. That is a policy we will scrap at the first opportunity.

At the heart of the Government's Budget strat-

egy is its utter abdication of any responsibility for

a wage-price policy. It has no such policy and no

constructive approach to reform of our industrial

relations system. Instead, it continues to harangue

the unions to accept further wage depression at

the same time. as it massively increases taxes,

interest rates, and health charges.

.

The Treasurer's foolish behaviour in this regard
must guarantee more avoidable and unnecessary industrial conflict in the year ahead. Confrontation with the unions will achieve nothing. Cer" tainly, after this Budget, the Government has no moral authority to insist on wage depression.

What must be attempted is a more creative and less divisive approach to industrial relations. We would propose that such measures include a thorough overhaul of the arbitration system including constitutional change by referendum to allow Australia to have a unified system of industrial regulation, including conciliation andarbitration. We would also abolish the Industrial Relations Bureau, repeal legislation designed to

harass and provoke industrial disputes and, in cooperation with the trade unions, develop a wagesprices policy to encompass prices, wage incomes, non-wage incomes, the social wage, taxation reform and elimination of tax avoidance. The Prime Minister can no longer rely on the tactics of open warfare with the union movement. His Government must attempt the measures we propose as creative and conciliatory if we are to avoid increasing industrial conflict.
Six years ago the Prime Minister promisedamongst a lot of other things-prosperity for all. Two years later, he told us that we were just about to stride into a new era of prosperity and development. Another three years later, he explained that-despite appearances-we were still poised on the threshold of this new era. The trouble is that, for most Australians, all this waiting has been a painful business. The average family is now about $17 Ii week worse off. The costs of food and other essential goods have continued to rise. Taxes are higher than they have ever been. Unemployment has doubled. The cost of homes is beyond the reach of most wage earners.
We know that the Treasurer does not spend much time in the supermarkets; his wife has told us so. And we all know that the Prime Minister is not renowned for mixing with the hoi polloLThat is a'pity. If the pair of them spent more. time in supermarkets, perhaps they might be better able to appreciate how their actions threaten the people they affect-the threat to their jobs and those of their children; the threat of ever increasing prices; the threat of losing their homes; the throat to their living standards; and most of all, the threat to their self-respect and personal dignity.
The. truth is that after six years of this kind of struggle and hardship~six years of being preached at and hectored about life not being easy-Australians deserve more than the squalid and venal conditions to which society has been reduced by the Fraser Government. We know that tens of thousands of people are literally sleeping in parks and railway stations because they have no homes. We know about the jobless and the homeless-including teenagers-who live in despair and on poverty benefits.

Mr HAYDEN-Exactly. We know the shocking and debilitating social and economic conditions of many Aboriginal Australians. We know the struggle of aged pensioners trying to survive on less than one-quarter of the average weekly wage. We in the Labor Party despise this insipid attitude. We believe we have the strong basis of a decent and just society in this country-a society that respects, trusts, and honours all its citizens. We have the basis here fora society of progress and prosperity-benefits that should accrue to all our people. We can ignore this potential. We can wait for others to use it and take its rewards. what the alternatives are. They are the same tired

interest rates through the roof.

Secondly, we see the same tired old solution to social problems. There is only one solution says

the Labor Party-massive increases in government spending, which can do no more than increase the taxation burden on the ordinary wage earner in Australia whom the Labor Party continually and falsely claims it represents. The third theme which is so evident in the speech from the Leader of the Opposition, is that the Labor Party's alternative policies would lead to nothing other than increased income tax for all sections of the community. No conclusion can be drawn other than that. A further theme of the Labor Party policy is that it would impose new taxes and a heavy taxation burden on development in this country. Let there be no mistake about it; it is opposed to development. The Labor Party has opposed all the development projects that have been mooted in Australia in recent years. There should be no doubt at all that the policy of the Labor Party that is presented as an alternative is more imposition of taxation on development within this country. Again, we hear the same tired old theme-technology and technological advance are bad. The Labor Party is in complete opposition to any support or encouragement of technological progress and development in this country which probably, more than anything else, has within it the hope for greatly expanded employment opportunities.
A common theme comes up every time the socialists put forward their alternative platforms. That is what it is-socialism, pure and simple. The Australian people should have no doubt about that whatsoever. Listening to the Leader of Opposition, I was reminded of his speech to the Fabian Society in Victoria, I think earlier this year. He declared without any equivocation that the objective of the Labor Party was to bring about mOre collectively owned goods and services in Australia. If that is the sort of system Labor Patty members want, they should move somewhere else because the Australian people do not want a system of government or a society which is based upon more collectively owned goods and services. Let there be no doubt about it. The lines are drawn between the progressive policies and programs outlined by the Treasu'rer (Mr Howard) in his Budget last week and this socialist program which has been outlined by the Leader of the Opposition tonight.
I want to speak tonight about the Budget, particularly those aspects that impinge upon my own areas of responsibility. It can be said without any doubt that the 1981-82 Budget represents a further example of the Government's determination to ensure that the conditions necessary for continued and balanced economic growth are

738 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

maintained. The area of my concern is the economy and the labour market. The last two financial years have seen some very hard won fruits of this Government's economic strategy. There has been a sustained strengthening of private sector activity with marked increases in business investment. Inflation fell further' in 1980-81 and remained below the average of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development countries. The overall result was a 4 per cent increase in real wages in 1980-81. This combination of economic achievement has led to a considerable inprovement in employment and a reduction in unemployment.
Especially encouraging has been the significant improvement in the labour market, as I have said, as a result of those economic achievements. Over the last two-year period, we have seen strong annual employment growth averaging about 2t per cent. In 1980-81, in particular, total employment grew strongly by 2.7 per cent during the year with full time employment increasing at a very healthy rate of 2.2 per cent or 114,000 jobs. Let there be no doubt whatsoever about the number of jobs that have been generated by this economic expansion and development. In the last two years to July 1981, employment has increased by 321,000 or 5.3 per cent. The employment growth during the year was widely based. It was not confined to one area. Good increases occurred in both male and female employment. Teenage employment rose significantly. There were rises in employment in all States with Queensland, Western Australia, New South Wales and the Northern Territory recording especially strong growth. The growth was also broadly based across industries and significant rises in employment occurred in the metal industries, finance, property and business services, community services and the construction and mining industries.
We hear nonsense from the Leader of the Opposition about the unemployment rate. Let us look at some very basic figures \Yhich will indicate the improvement. We say again tonight, as we have said on every occasion when this subject has been debated, that the unemployment levels are, of course, completely unsatisfactory. But we point out that in July 1979 the full time unemployment rate was 5.9 per cent; last year it was 5.8 per cent; and this year it is 5.6 per cent. The number of unemployed young people dropped in the year. to July 1981 by 10,000. Again we emphasise that this is not good enough and that there is a .serious problem in the unemployment field. But that problem will not be remedied by any means other than sustained economic growth. It is only sustained economic growth which will generate

through the economy the jobs that are needed in Australia in the large numbers in which they are needed. It is important that the economic and labour market gains of the last two years, as I have just outlined, are not frittered away; and wrong policies can lead, of course, to exactly that.
The undedying aim of the Budget is to consolidate the process of economic recovery which is already apparent in Australia. It does this by setting itself squarely against a possible resurgence of inflationary pressure. The experience of Australia in the early 1970s, and that of many Western industrial countries, shows that there is a link between wage levels and unemployment. I made the point this afternoon-it is a basic, although to some people unpalatable, truth-that people will employ other people when it is profitable and economic to do so and when the potential employees have the skills, the ability and the experience that employers want.

It should be recognised that an acceleration of wage inflation, particularly at this time, would impact not only on prospects for future growth in employment, but on the distribution of the jobs which are available. Who would be the sufferers in that situation? The unemployed would be the
sufferers. The young people would be the sufferers. Unions, in making their demands for increased wages, and the Australian Labor Party
in this Parliament, in encouraging them in that endeavour, should remember exactly that-that it is the innocent, who suffer and it is the unemployed who will not be able to fill the jobs which cannot be created by means other than economicgrowth.

Fortunately, the recent steps that have been

taken by this Government to bring the relevant

industrial relations parties together appear to

have led to a calmer approach to wage determi-

nation. There is a recognition now that the aban-

donment of indexation has not left a vacuum and

need not create chaos. The conciliation and arbi-

tration system is still there to facilitate orderly

wage settlements which are within the interests of

the economy and the community and which can

enable the economy to develop and expand but

which will not intrude those inflationary forces

Which can lead only to the destruction of the

economy, the destruction of jobs and the dest,ruc-

tion of the hopes of the unemployed and of those

YOllng people with whom we concerned ourselves

,in a debate earlier today and who are looking for

jobs.

'"

.

It is essential that the parties to the industrial relations system exercise restraint and that the recent wave of industrial unrest is not repeated. It is another very basic principle about this matter that

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 739

employers will not take on extra employees when there is so much uncertainty in the industrial sphere. They will not take on extra employees when they have no guarantee that goods will be delivered or services can be provided. They ~ill not take on extra employees when there is no guarantee that they will be able to carry out their business and expand their business without continual industrial disputation. Those uncertainties inevitably cloud the outlook for the labour market. In line with the forecast for economic growth, however, the Budget estimates are for an employment growth of 2 per cent in this year. This growth rate, of course, is lower than that experienced in the last two years, but it compares more than favourably with the experiences of the mid1970s, when employment growth averaged less than one per cent per year.
The manpower programs and the services provided by the Department of Employment and Youth Affairs make a positive contribution to the expansion of employment within Australia. I said earlier that one of the main determinants of whether employers wi\l employ people is whether it is profitable and economic to do so. The other determinant, of course, is whether the people that are available have the skills, the ability and the experience that the employer wants. It is in that latter area that manpower policies and programs make such a powerful and substantial contribution to employment. These programs are an important complement to the economic and social policies of the Government and within this overall environment they have an important part to play in improving the efficiency of the labour market, particularly the adaptibility and suitability of the supply of labour and the employment experience of particular disadvantaged groups. Put simply, if employers cannot get the people they need or workers the jobs they want, the consequences will be costly indeed for this country and for individuals. They will be costly in terms of higher unemployment, in terms of lost economic growth and in terms of inflationary 'Wage pressures. One would hope that there would be bipartisan and universal support for the encouragement and development of those programs.
Manpower and training programs are an im~ portant means of addressing two current issues of major public concern in-the labour market: Firstly, youth unemployment problems, which remain despite some recent improvement, and, secondly, the shortages of skilled labour, which are becoming increasingly apparent. There is, not unnaturally, confusion about how these problems can exist together. There is, quite rightly, a concern that everything that can be done should be

done to train Australians for job opportunities generated by economic growth. The Government's commitment to manpower and training programs becomes a reality through the placement services of the Commonwealth Employment Service. People are able to use the Commonwealth Employment Service to get the jobs they need and advice on the range of employment and training programs available to assist them. It becomes a reality through specific programs of assistance directed at both trade and non-trade skills training. It becomes a reality through ensuring that young people have access to practical programs aimed directly at providing work experience and training opportunities.
The 1981-82 Budget represents an increased emphasis on manpower and training programs to provide, in particular, practical and positive help for young unemployed people and to increase the supply of skilled workers needed by industry. There has been an increase in the allocations to all employment and youth affairs programs to $203m in 1981-82. Within that total, expenditure on manpower and training programs will increase by nearly $50m, or 31 per cent over last year, to $197m. So there has been a very substantial increase in the expenditure committed by the Government to manpower and training programs and an expansion of the number of places available to individuals under those programs.
This increase in expenditure will provide in particular for the many new initiatives which the Government has introduced to assist those young people experiencing difficulty in obtaining employment. In 1981-82 this emphasis will continue through work experience programs, the expenditure on which will be increased from $41.3m to $65.1m, a 58 per cent increase over the last financial year. There is available a higher level of subsidy to assist longer term unemployed young people to obtain work experience over a 34-week period. This measure was introduced in February 1981 and it is expected that some 12,000 young people will benefit in the present financial year. The transition allowance was also introduced in February 1981 to provide an incentive for young unemployed people to return to relevant education and training courses in the technical and further education institutions. Expenditure on this will increase from $4.7m to $13m. It is estimated that this will enable some 16,000 young people to take advantage of the expansion in the school-to-work transition program which the Commonwealth has set as its objective in 1982.
It should now be plain, as a result of the debate which has taken place over the last few days concerning the Community Youth Support Scheme,

740 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No. J)

that the Government has a very firm and clear position with respect to its obligations towards young people. In the first place it has an awareness of that obligation and an awareness of their immediate and very substantive needs. But it should also be clear that we are now firmly on the path where we believe that what we as a government can do best, in the first place, is to ensure there is a lively, vibrant and expanding economy, which in the final analysis is the only way in which jobs can be generated within the economy. Secondly, we can make a substantial contribution' by directing young people to those situations where they can acquire the skills, the training and the work experience which they need to acq.uire and keep jobs.

There are no short cuts to employment. There are no easy solutions. It is a matter which requires very sensible thought and serious consideration. That is the reason why a great deal of thought has been put into the programs that my Department administers. It is the reason why we believe we are on the right track now. We say the emphasis now must be put on skills training, on young people acquiring work experience. We say that the money is there because of the increased commitments by the Government in this Budget. The places are there because of the increased and expanded range of opportunities for young people under
those programs. We say that if young people are directed to and advised of those areas where they
can take part in programs they will be able to acquire the skills which they need.

Let us look at all the strategies we have seen. When Mr Fraser came to power we had the investment-led recovery. After that it was the consumer-led recovery. Honourable members will remember Charlie Court's idea at the Premiers Conference about the wages-prices freeze. Then we had the wages-prices freeze for three weeks, which fell apart. Then there was a tax-cut recovery. People were told to ring up and find out how much their tax cuts were. The cuts were given in February and were taken away in July. Then, without any mandate, we had the $3,000m petrol tax. Then it got round to the big fix, the sell out to the foreigners, the resources boom. The Government opposed the move at the Arbitration Commission and because there was ajump in retail sales and because the Government's opposition was ignored by the Arbitration Commission we got a little growth.
The money supply jumped the rails. The Government has a tight monetary policy, which keeps growth and the money supply down. It is the old Chicago school line. Try to starve them out and hold inflation down. What happened? The money supply blew up. There was some money around so people invested in buildings. Home units, fiats and houses were built and the building industry picked up and things started to go again. Property started to move. People said: 'Maybe there is a bit of growth around.' But all these things happen despite the Government, not because of the Government. It is the Government which argues for a tight monetary policy, not for a loose one. Mr BRADFIELD (Barton) (9.22)-Before moving to some of the positive aspects of this Budget--
Mr Armitage-Which ones?
Mr BRADFIELD-If the honourable member stays there he will hear them. Firstly I will place in context some of the arguments put forward by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden). I am not sure whether the Leader of the Opposition is a magician or believes in fairy tales. When one analyses in depth the economic arguments put forward by him, they just do not add up. The Leader of the Opposition played heavily on the poverty of people. We realise that some people are living in poverty in Australia. This Government is concerned about that. To correct poverty is not a matter of going out and, with two hands, handing out dollar notes to people; it goes a lot deeper than that. Certainly this Government is interested in correcting that situation. It is not as easy as the Leader of the Opposition pointed out.
Let us look atsome of the Leader of the Opposition's main economic measures and his suggestions. He said that if the Opposition were in power it would abandon the sales tax increases just imposed in this Budget, including an amount" of $500m. He said that income tax would be reduced but he did not say by how much or how. In looking at some of the areas where this money would come from he said that he would eliminate the ongoing investment allowance, bring in a resource tax and give free health care to everyone: Back in Labor's reign from 1972 to 1975, free health care for everyone cost approximately $2,OOOm. Goodness knows what it would cost today. It might be all right in principle, but in practice it is an absolute impossibility. It's not possible to give everyone everything for nothing.
The Leader of the Opposition then went on to say that he would eliminate tax avoidance. His Government has no record of showing any sincerity towards eliminating the tax avoidance industry in Australia. The only Government that has shown any sincerity towards this is the Liberal-National Country Party Government. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) delivered his Budget Speech only last week. He has done more to eliminate tax avoidance in this country than any other

Treasurer or any collection of Treasurers. Mr Deputy Speaker, tax avoidance in this country is coming to an end. That means a better share of taxation amongst all Australians.
The Leader of the Opposition tried to put a popular choice to the people without having regard to economic reality. I could not help but formulate a picture in my mind as he was delivering his speech of a man standing in the middle of a busy supermarket handing out dollar notes with both hands. In that situation one would have throngs of people around who were willing to take dollar notes, saying, 'Good on you, mate.' When they walked away they would think, 'What a fool that fellow was. Why would anyone be foolish enough to stand there and give me a dollar note for nothing?' In the middle of his speech the Leader of the Opposition said something which showed the fine economic record this Government has.
The honourable member for Blaxland (Mr Keating) criticised the economic policies of this Government, yet the Leader of the Opposition stated that if he were in government he would run a mild deficit. He went on to say that this country can afford it. If this country can afford a' mild deficit that, in itself, is proof of the economic situation created since this Government came to power in 1975; the fact is that the economy has improved and is sound.
At Budget time the country seems to go into mild turmoil. People speculate before the presentation of the Budget as to what could happen. When the Budget comes out everything is written about the good and the bad of it. This Budget is a little different. There was not a lot of Budget speculation before the event. When one analyses what has been said in the media, there has not been a lot of wild speculation. I take that to be an acceptance by the Australian people that deep down, even though the Budget may not have a lot of goodies in it, even though it was not a Budget handing out dollar notes with both hands, they realise. that the Budget echoes the continually improving economy of Australia, an economy from which everyone will benefit.
Let us look at some of the economic activities, economic indicators and inflation. Inflation gets a mention all the time because it is so important. It is important to our future, to investment and to all the people of Australia. Many people have forgotten how they were hurt when, in 1972 to 1975, inflation was running at 17 per cent. Many pensioners have forgotten how their pension payments were eroded and were losing their buying power. You would know, Mr Deputy Speaker,

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 745

that with a high rate of inflation not Qnly is there eCQnomic instability, and an unwillingness Qn the part of the people to invest, but also it makes the difference between the rich and the poor greater. If one happens to have assets those assets appreciate with inflatiQn. The people who do not have many assets or who. have no assets at all lose out cQmpletely.
In 1972 to 1975 that is exactly what happened. The gap between those that had it and those who did not became greater. That is why this Government is concerned to contrQl inflation and places it as No. 1 in priority. That is the way to. reduce the gap between the rich and the poor. Among the Qther indicators is our balance of payments. It has been very strong and that has been recQgnised by Qverseas investors. That is why the Australian dollar is so strong on the international currency markets and that is why people are wanting to invest in Australia.
The investment indicator alone shows the tremendous confidence that these peQple have in our co~qtry-a confidence that has been created by thi~Government. I know that members of the Opposition detest foreign investment. I know they have some magical theory about stopping foreign investment which perhaps they think is appealing to. the Australian people. They talk of buying back the farm and about buying back Australia; but nowhere do they say where this mQney will come from. There. is no. magic pot of money in Australia. There is no huge uninvested sum of money that belongs to Australians. If we stop foreign investment, we stop a tremendous part Qf Australia's expansion. We must make sure that foreign investment is controlled and that Australia is treated fairly by those peQple who want to invest here because they feel confident about our economy. We must ensure that we get our fair share of taxation from thQse people investing in Australia and we must also be sure that we always exercise sufficient control Qver those people.
Mr Deputy President, we can look at the business surveys. An Australia and New Zealand Banking GrQup Ltd survey speaks in glowing terms of the Australian economy. Surveys conducted QVer the last two Qr three years by all sorts of business organisations have all come up with one finding: The business outlook on the future Qf Australia is sound. Let us lQok at what has happened over the years to the increased competitiveness of Australian manufactured goods. The fact is that we have a textile industry that is on the way back up again. It was destroyed during the years 1972-75. It is the sound economy that has given these industries the impetus to get up off the

floor again and be world competitivecQmpetitive with the costs of Qther countries that years ago we had no hope Qf matching. Looking at the employment statistics mentioned by the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) who preceded me in this debate, we see that Qver Qne million more people are emplQyed in Australia tQday than were employed in 1975 when we came into office. That is quite an achievement. At the same time, unemployment has gone down, and it has gone down while we have absorbed 130,000 new schoolleavers a year. When Qne looks at the indicators, one sees that the Australian eCQnomy is not in bad shape. I purposely leave until last among those indicatQrs interest rates. The honourable member for Blaxland mentiQned them; the Leader of the Opposition mentiQned them.
Mr Uren-I will mention them too.
Mr BRADFIELD-The honourable member for Reid will mention them tQO. Interest rates are at a level which is not acceptable to this Government or to the people of Australia. The Australian people deserve some sort of explanation why interest rates have come up and why we must do our best to return them to a mQre acceptable level. Throughout this economic activity, there has been an increased demand for mQney, for home construction, industry and the development of our resources. We have become unbalanc~d. We have reached the stage at which too many people want to borrow money and not enough people are willing to lend money. That is also unacceptable. That cannot continue. We cannot continually allow only the privileged to. get money. The only way in which to bring that situation back into order is to. let interest rates come up to a realistic level, a level that perhaps deters some people from borrowing money unnecessarily, and a level which at the same time encourages more people to lend mQney.
Another serious situation that has cQntributed greatly to the unacceptable rise in interest rates over the last 18 months has been the greedy borrowing by State governments, local governments and semi-government authorities. They go to the market, regardless of what interest rates are available, and they pay them. The reason they pay these rates is that directly or indirectly the public is paying their interest bills. When the electricity commission of whatever State it may be wants to borrow mQney, it pays its 14 per cent or 15 per cent and then it loads the cost Qn to the public by way of charges. The Government in my State Qf New South Wales is a prime example. When it wants money to go ahead with a program it pays the ruling in~erest rate. It charges that coSt back to

746 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

the people of New South Wales. So, the greed of State governments and of semi-government authorities has been a great contributor to interest rates. When the Premier of New South Wales complains about this matter at Premiers confer" ences, his own conscience must tell him that he has been the number one contributor to rising interest rates in Australia because his Government has been prepared to pay the rate. If people are not prepared to pay high rates, interest rates will not go up.
Let us look at some of the advantages of this Budget and at some of the measures which show that we care for people. The family allowance is to be generously increased. This is an important increase that goes to the heart of our Australian way of life because there is no more important unit in Australia than the family. This increase in family allowance helps by way of an additional $13 a month for the third child and the same amount for the fourth child. Let us look ,at the rebate of 32c in the dollar for hospital cover. That is an assistance to those people who are paying these fees and who need private hospital cover. This probably has not been fully ,realised yet because we have not reached the end of the tax year when people get the advantage of that 32c in the dollar rebate. A matter that has been mentioned rarely since the Budget was presented is the, increase in the tax free threshold of personal income tax. It has increased from $4,041 per annum to $4,195 per annum. At the same time the rate of increase in the income tax scale also has been adjusted. People are just starting to realise the, generosity of the adjustment to the spouse allQwance for income tax purposes. The spouse allowanc,e was recently increased from $597 per annum to $800 per annum. That is a rebate-a discount-off one's tax. It is not a deduction or a reduction of taxable income; it is far more important than that. That $203 extra rebate is the equivalent of increasing the tax free threshold of a person's income by $635. When one makes calculations regarding a one income family; one will see that the breadwinner of that family is now able to earn $6,695 per, annum or $130 a week before the taX: scales apply. People have not yet fully realised the great relief to Australian families that that increase, in the spouse allowance will mean.
There is a matter in this Budget which I reg~rd as a milestone for the Australian people and, for the, Government. We virtually ,have anend tei the deficit-adeficifthat has been plaguingAustraHa since 1974 and thIS Gcivernmentsince it caine to office. No longer do we have this worry. The deficit virtually has gone. that is quite an achievement. Anyone \Vho looks at the'accumUlation of

annual deficits that this Gover,nment has had to cope with over the years will see that it totals $20 billion. That is money that has been borrowed from somewhere. It is economic stupidity for the Leader of the Opposition to get up anQ say that it is all right to fI~n at a deficit. The people of Australia, through the government of Australia, have to, get that money frQm somewhere. The fact that there is no deficit in the Budget framed by the Treasurer last week is quite an achievement. It is an achIevement that heralds future relief for all AustraliaJ}s. No longer'do we need to obtain revenue to cope with a deficit. This means that less money will have to be collected from the Australian people in the revenue collection area in the future;
Finally, let me take this Parliament back to the, Speech ,o( the Governor-General at the opening of this Parliament. In that Speech the Governor General said that this Government placed great recognition on the individual Australian. This Governme\lt does place great recognition on the indiviQual Australian. That is why, through the Budget, we have brought about economic stability. That iswhy in the future the individual Australian can look forward to greater prosperity and a lower taxation base. I applaud the Budget. It heralds economic stability for Australia and ensures our' future.

Mr ARMITAGE (Chifiey) (9.42)-The honourable member for Barton (Mr Bradfield) talked a while ago of the efforts of this Government in relation to tax avoidance. He neglected to rnention, of course, that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) and the Minister for Industry and Cominerce(Sir Phillip Lynch) were among the people who had family trusts and who, until the Government altered the laws of this country, were some of the prIze tax avoiders in this country. In other words, it is one thing to talk of ending tax avoidance and it is another 'thing to put it into practice. We must recall at all times that this Government is a Government of greed before need.
'The"honourable member for Barton just sh6we~ his complete lack of understanding of national accounts. when he talked of deficit b~dgeting. He forgot; of course, that this Government is a great llupporter ()f the United States of 1\m~rica. The honourabl,e member for Barton apparently does not' even realise that the Budget deficit inthat country ranges from $50 billion to

Appropriation Bill (No. J)

25 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 747

$75 billion, not millions of dollars. If the honourable member sat down and started to learn a little about national accounts he might not make such extraordinary claims. As I said, this Government is a government of greed before need. It is a government which has brought forward a nogrowth Budget, a Budget which is deliberately setting out to bring about higher unemployment and is deliberately setting out to bring about a redistribution of income from the lower and lower middle income groups to the higher income groups. This policy is being followed very deliberately by this Government of greed before need.
I start off by looking at what is happening to young people today. Only this afternoon we witnessed some of the greatest exhibitions ever in this Parliament in the debate about the abolition of the Community Youth Support Scheme. The Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) apparently did not even know that this was to happen as he went around the sticks saying how he would provide more insofar as CYSS is concerned. A new arrangement was to be introduced, but along came Budget night and the Treasurer (Mr Howard) said: 'Sorry, CYSS is abolished'. Even the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs did not know about it.
The young married couples who are starting out in life and getting their first homes are the people who are being hit harder than probably any other section of the community by the deliberate policies of this Government on interest rates. I am seeing examples of it all around me. The newly married young people who are getting their first homes are copping it harder than anybody. This applies particularly to the young people who have a first mortgage, a second mortgage and a personal loan from a credit union or bank. I have seen examples of this very close to my own family. I understand what is going on with these young people. They just cannot afford the continual increases in their monthly payments brought about by the increase in interest rates. The dream of every young person of owning his own home is becoming more and more an impossi. bility because of the deliberate policies of this Government.
Let us look at the question of family allowances. For the first time since 1976-for the first time in five years-there has been an increase in family allowances for the third and subsequent children in a family. But the young people who are starting to build their families and who have one child or two children will not get a razoo extra. They will still receive the rates of five years ago. If ever a thimble and pea trick was performed

it was performed five years ago when the Government said: 'All right, instead of giving you a tax rebate for your children we will give it to you as afamily allowance increase. The women will get it. That way the men will not get to spend it.' That is the way the Government went about it. For a start it did not even give the equivalent of the tax rebate in the way of an increase in the family allowance. Secondly, whereas the tax rebate would have been indexed time and again since then, the family allowance has not been indexed. In other words, as I said, it was a thimble and pea trick. We say that we want the young Australians to have families. It is when they are starting off their families and when they are starting to buy their own homes that they need assistance and that is the very time that they are not getting the assistance.
I will not be purely negative in this regard and say that this is purely because of the Government's deliberate policies of increasing interest rates. Let us face reality and accept that the interest rate situation has got to the stage of the dog chasing the tail. Because of the continual increases in interest rates, people expect those rates to increase even further. Therefore, they wi1l not put their money into long term securities. They put their money instead into the short term money market because they know that they can get at it quickly and go back and reinvest as the rates increase time and time again. It becomes an issue of expectation of increased interest rates, of the dog chasing the tail. Yet the Treasurer says that he thinks that this will be the last time that they will increase. That is what he said the last time. It wi1l happen again and again until this Government alters its policies.
Accepting the need, first of all, to control the increase in the money supply-an increase which has gone Jar beyond the Budget estimate of last year; once again the Government's indications in the Budget were completely ill-inFormed-and accepting the need to control inflation and, in particular, the inflation in house prices, which is making it harder and harder for our younger people to obtain their first homes, what policies ought to be followed? What should be done? first of all, the Government should utilise its existing powers under the Banking Act and get the Reserve Bank to call in statutory reserve deposits; in other words, cream off excess funds from the trading banks and put them into the Reserve Bank through the use of its existing powers under the Banking Act. Secondly, why has the Government not proclaimed the whole of the Financial Corporations Act introduced by the Whitlam Government. The first section of it was proclaimed in order to get the necessary statistics.

'Lake and Stream' Radio Program-Interest Rates-Community Youth Support Scheme-Education--Sales Tax

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! It being 10 p.m., under sessional order. I propose the question:
That the House do now adjourn.
Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (1O.0)-There is a saying that old fisherpersons never die, they just smell that way. A very similar sort of fishy smell emanates from the circumstances surrounding the Canberra Australian Broadcasting Commission's axing in mid July of the very popular fishing radio program called Lake and Stream. Nearly four years ago, the program was conceived by Graham Pike, a highly experienced angler and journalist. Since then, twice a week on ABC radio 2CN Canberra he has informed, interested and involved a conservatively estimated total of six million listeners in fish, their lake and stream habitats and associated environmental matters. The Lake and Stream program was as popular as ever when suddenly it was cast out by the ABC in Canberra last month. There is sufficient evidence to show that the program was the indirect victim of the pressures the Fraser Government has applied and is applying to the ABC generally. It was a victim of the conservatism, perhaps even paranoia, increasing within the ABC in response to these Government pressures. The ABC is as frightened of this Government as a trout would be of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) stumbling down the middle of a creek in bright blue, striped waders. Worse still, it appears that at least some ABC management and middle management officers feel they should curry favour not only with government but with our rod wielding international angler, the Prime Minister himself.
What I am saying is supported by the background to the demise of Mr Pike's Lake and Stream program. Several times in the past, Mr Pike has taken a: satirical cast at the Prime Minister, without any offence being meant of course and apparently without it being taken by the Prime Minister. Certainly the local ABC management of those times did not even raise and eyebrow. Then, fairly recently, there came to the ABC a change of management and with it a new negative attitude to Mr Pike's fishing program. This new attitude first found expression about seven months ago in a memo from the ABC Canberra management to 2CN technical staff instructing them that they were not to record Lake and Stream or put it to air before Mr Pike's script had been vetted and. approved. by the program supervisor of radio. The vetting soon became.censorship as parts of the script of the sort which were quite often used. in the program's first three years were cut out.

750 REPRESENTATIVES 25 August 1981

Adjournment

On 26 June this year, Mr Pike opened the Lake and Stream program by jocularly suggesting that the Prime Minister's visit to North America at that time amounted to a fishing holiday. That program had already been substantially censored by ABC middle management before it went to air. Mr Pike was told that despite what his research had shown, it was not proper to satirise the Prime Minister. Further, a joke at the Prime Minister's expense was ordered to be cut. Even taken out of the context of the program's script, it is hard to see why the ABC's management objected to the joke. The joke had it that President Reagan was going to compare his fan club membership with that of Mr Fraser. It was suggested that Mr Fraser would be at some disadvantage. President Reagan's list included the entertainers Bob Hope, Johnny Cash and Stevie Wonder. However, on the Prime Minister's fan club list, there was no hope, no cash and no wonder. Despite the fact that this joke was censored and the program that day further watered down, Mr Pike was told in a hand delivered letter nearly immediately after the program went to air that Lake and Stream was to be dropped by 2CN. The reason given in the letter was that the ABC did not have enough money to keep the program going. It -was costing $35 per week. Mr Pike then offered to do the program free of charge to save the ABC this huge amount. But after two days' consideration, management replied that if the ABC accepted free programs, it would not appear to the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair), that the ABC was hurting from the Budget cuts and the ABC wanted to appear to be hurting from those cuts.
Since Lake and Stream has been dropped by 2CN there has been much public opposition to the ABC's decision and management has given in writing several different reasons for its actions, none of them, however, listing money as a reason. I close with a warning: People may well wonder what the end result will ~e for the ABC and for themselves if the sort of censorship to which a fishing program has been subjected is extended to other programs or, worse still, is allowed to occur at all within the ABC.
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.5)-On a previous occasion in the adjournment debate, the honourable member forHotham (Mr Kent) chose to read to us a very interesting letter from one of his constituents relating to home ownership and interest rates. It is quite strange that I now have the opportunity to read a letter that I received today. The letter states:
Dear Wilson,
Mildred and I have been reading the paper about interest rates. You blokes are sure getting some Hack. I feel very sorry

for the young ones with the big mortgages and I hope you can help them. However, compared to me they at lea,st have a job and their unions to keep pushing up their pay. Sometimes I wonder if the young ones expect too much. When our Jenny married young Bob they bought a block of land before the engagement ring. That house of theirs has got everything in it you know, and Jenny had to keep her car after they got married. Anyway, Bob says he's not worried. He says he could make a tax free $20,000 on the place already. Well, good luck to them. Bob is a good worker. What I wanted to tell you about was Mildred and me. Don't shout it too loud, but your interest rate policy has just brought us back above the brea.d line. You remember the settlement I got in '75 after the accident-$65,OOO. Well, I got the part pension too. You know Mildred, I might have taken a risk but she said only sure investments. We put half into the Australian savings bonds and the rest into savings investments with the Commonwealth Bank. When we worked it out, things looked pretty good. In 1976 we. averaged 8 per cent interest-about $5;200 for the year. Now we had to pay some income tax and there was still a bit on the house, but it worked out that the pension covered most of that and we could have our daily living from the interest. Mildred said we would never touch the capital and we haven't, not that it will buy much now. But I'll tell you something, while you blokes were keeping interest rates down for the home buyers and the Government you sure made it hard for us. Mildred and I sat down with some old bills the other night-you know how she keeps the accounts to the cent. Well we worked out that in 1976 our living cost was about 90 bucks a week and that was $4,680 a year. That would be about right too as we had a bit over that year. So even though I would never be able to work again everything looked okay. The 90 bucks included a few beers and the odd night out and petrol for the car. Anyway, so as not to bore you, we also added it up for the nex t few years. 1977, we got the same interest-$5,200, but our living costs went up to $5,400. In 1978 interest rates went down a bit. We averaged 7.5 per cent which was $4,875 for the year. But our living costs went up to $6,000, that is, if we lived that way as we did in '77. I know this because we woke up early in the year and had to cut back, had to still use some of Mildred's savings to make do. In 1979 we got 7.75 per cent or $5,037, but by calculation, our living costs for the same things were $6,500. Boy, did I get cranky not having a drink. Didn't buy too many clothes either. Well, things are a bit better now and I've just written out the figures. 1980 the interest rate was 8.75 per cent for a return of $5,687 but by then our estimated cost of living was $7,049 for the same things. In 1981 it went up further to $7,718. So you can see we've caught up a bit but ifinHation is $10,000 this year it will still be $600 short of where I was in 1976. Okay mate, we haven't starved but I think you've got a responsibility to people who've saved a bit for a rainy day to see they earn a reasonable return on their savings. Mildred and I aren't getting any younger and really I should get a bit more pension as you haven't changed the means test business on my earnings for a lot of years. Hope you're enjoying Parliament and keep up the good work but make sure they know there are two sides to the story. By the way, if you can get inHation down we'll be happy to take less interest.
Bert
